Prolect And Credit Summary
borrower:            Islamic Republic of Pakistan
Beneficiaries:       Province of Balochistan
Province of the Punjab
Federal Ministry of Health
Amount:              SDR 35.0 million (US$48.0 million equivalent)
Terns:               Standard, with 35 years maturity
Project
Description:        The project has the main objective of improving the health
status of the population of the two provinces through
assisting with the implementation of the provincial health
development  programs.    These  programs  consist  of  the
investments to be made during the Eighth Five-Year
Development Plan, plus a number of agreed policy measures.
The programs are consistent with the Social Action Program
(SAP) under preparation by the Government. The SAP aims at
improving the performance of social services through
ensuring better implementation of ongoing activities, proper
budgeting of recurrent expenditures and proper planning of
development  expenditures  according  to  implementation
capacity.  The health programs of the two provinces will
increase the emphasis on (a) primary health, and (b)
preventive and promotive services. The project will finance
key  parts  of  the  programs  concentrating  on:    (a)
strengthening primary health services from the village to
the district level, focusing on improved maternal and child
health services; (b) staff development, focusing on improved
staff capabilities and performance and increasing the number
of female paramedical staff; and (c) management and
organizational development, focusing on improved management
capabilities. There are also limited federal inputs. The
project will finance:   construction  and  expansion  of
training facilities and limited health facility upgrading;
furniture, equipment and transport; contraceptives; training
costs; technical assistance; and incremental recurrent costs
including medicines. It will be implemented by the federal
and provincial governments, local government and cooperating
NGOs with domestic and international technical support. The
project will be implemented on the basis of annual agreed
sub-programs which, inter alia, will seek to ensure



(ii)
appropriate balance between recurrent and investment
financing and would allow for adjustments in work plans
based on previous years accomplishments.
Benefits and Risks: The project will improve the health status of the population
generally but with a special emphasis on high risk and
under-privileged sections of the community. They will have
improved access to services of a higher quality and of
increased effectiveness. Women will benefit, particularly
through improved maternal health services, as will children.
For example, about 20X of pregnant women will receive some
prenatal care and the number of supervised deliveries will
double. The government will benefit from the institutional
development components, which will also pave the way for
more ambitious objectives to be met through subsequent
investments.  The main risk is the limited implementation
capacity of the provincial Health Departments; this risk is
addressed   through   having   a   substantial   management
strengthening component, simplifying the project design, and
providing   for  technical   assistance   in  key  areas.
Strengthening public health systems and particularly
focusing on women's health problems including family
planning may not receive sufficient priority from the
present health system; this risk is minimized through
fostering strong comitment to the project from senior
officials concerned during project preparation, the project
reinforcing and financing key primary and maternal/child
care aspects of the government's health policy, increasing
the involvement of women in service delivery, and through
the  involvement  of  public  health  orientated  local
institutions.     Finally,  the  governments'  procedures
(including the PC-ls) may be too rigid to accommodate fully
the flexibility offered by the programmatic project
elements; this risk is addressed through including the
maximum flexibility in government project documents and
procedures, and introducing annual project reviews.

ISLANIC_REPUBLIC OF PAKISTAN
SECOND FAMILY HtALTH PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
I. SECTOR BACKGROUND. ISSUES AND CONSTRAINTS
A. Intoctign
1.    The Government of Pakistan has requested the Association to help finance
the Health Development Programs for the provinces of the Punjab and Balochistan.
This will be a follow up project to the Family Health Project benefiting the
provinces of Sindh and North-West Frontier (NWFP).1/  The programs for the
Punjab and Balochistan are the health components of the Eighth Five-Year
Development Plan (1993/94 to 1997/98). The main objective of these programs is
to improve the health status of the population in general but with a special
emphasis on high risk and underprivileged sections of the community such as the
poor, women and children. The strategy will include a series of investments and
measures to: (a) make health services more effective, efficient, affordable and
acceptable; (b) give greater priority to disease prevention, health promotion and
maternal and child health (MCH); (c) establish a better balance between urban and
rural services; and (d) improve community participation.
2.    The programs of the two provinces are fully in accordance with the Social
Action Program (SAP) being proposed by the government to ensure better
implementation of ongoing activities in the social sectors (education, health,
population and rural water supply and sanitation), to ensure proper budgeting of
recurrent expenditures for these sectors and to ensure appropriate development
expenditures consistent with implementation capacity and with financing available
in ongoing operations.
3.  The proposed project will support the activities of the government programs
by assisting with: (a) a shift of emphasis towards primary health care and away
from secondary and tertiary care; and (b) a shift of emphasis away from curative
care and towards preventive and promotive  services.   Within these broad
objectives the project will support the key areas of: (a) enhancing the provision
of maternal and child care; and (b) facilitating changes particularly through
strengthened training and management.
4.    The five year health development program of the two provinces is estimated
to cost US$520 million dollars equivalent. The proposed project will finance
US$114 million dollars equivalent including parallel financing from the British
Overseas Development Administration (ODA) and the German Kreditanstalt fur
Wiederaufbau (KfW).
1' Pakistan mily Health Project. Credit 2240-PA, awpproved by the Board an Ha 7, 1991



- 2 -
5.   The proposed project differs from conventional Bank projects in that it has
gone a long way towards adopting a programmatic approach based on sectoral
investments and a policy reform program, while disbursing on a selective part
of the overall program. Although providing a basic implementation schedule,
project implementation will be based on annual government/donor reviews which
will review the previous year's fiscal policy and financial implementation and
agree on fiscal policy and financial implementation for the following year.
B. Background2
6.    Pakistan's demographic and health characteristics are typical of many
developing countries: high fertility, low life expectancy, widespread infectious
and communicable diseases and high maternal and child mortality, coupled with a
young age structure and increasing urbanization.   While some progress in
addressing these problems has been made, health and demographic indicators remain
poor throughout the country.   In Balochistan, the position is particularly
serious while in the Punjab, indicators, although better, are still poor.
Economic and Social Setting
7.   For most of the 1980s, Pakistan's GDP grew at a high 6.5% p. a. in real
terms.   Despite this sustained growth, the economy has shown a number of
structural weaknesses and, in the late 1980s, inappropriate aggregate demand
policies resulted in a financial crisis. In reaction to the crisis, Pakistan has
been implementing a medium-term adjustment program, but with mixed results.
Since the current government took office in 1990, there has been rapid progress
in deregulation and privatization, GDP and export growth has strengthened, and
private sector investment and confidence has increased.   However, financial
balances have remained under pressure; in particular in 1991/92 the fiscal
deficit remained high at about 7% of GDP, while the external current account
deficit increased to US$2.5 billion (5.2% of GNP).  Despite the high fiscal
deficit, weak resource mobilization and competing demands on budgetary resources
from non-development expenditures (e.g. interest and defense expenditures) have
constrained the government's ability to alleviate infrastructure constraints,
improve social conditions and reduce poverty.
8.    Poverty remains widespread in Pakistan with, for example, the poorest 40%
of households spending US$100 per month or less. Poverty is more prevalent in
rural areas than in large cities3/. Socio-cultural barriers to improving health
status, particularly for women and children, are high and include very low female
literacy in rural areas (varying from 2% in Balochistan to 9% in the Punjab), a
largely rural population (about 80% in Balochistan and 65% in the Punjab) often
living in remote villages with poor communications, low status traditionally

the Poor to Basic Services in Pakistan, January 17, 1992 (Report No. 9826-PhA).



accorded to women and limitations on the movement of females outside the
household (especially strict in Balochistan).
C. Health and Po2ulation Sector Status
PoRmlation4/
9.    Pakistan's population of almost 120 million makes it the ninth largest
country in the world. The current population growth rate is estimated to be 3.1X
per annum, which is among the highest in Asia. This rate is the result of a
slow, steady decline in mortality and of little change in fertility. If this
rate of growth continues, the population will double in the next 23 years; much
of this growth is now unavoidable.
10.   Fertility and Family Planning.  The total fertility rate is estimated to
be at least 5.8 births per woman, which is again among the highest in Asia, and
the crude birth rate is about 42 per thousand population (1990).  Universal
marriage, low contraceptive use, and declining breastfeeding, contribute to
maintaining high fertility.  There is no evidence of a sustained start to a
general fertility decline despite significant unmet demand for family planning
services. Abortion is illegal in Pakistan except to save the life of the mother;
little is known about the extent of illegal abortion. The shortcomings of past
government population efforts, which operated in a hostile social environment,
include: (a) frequent changes in administration and organization; (b) a family
planning supply orientation with weak demand creation; (c) inadequate evaluation;
(d) unrealistic target setting leading to low staff morale; (e) shifting
political commitment; and (f) insufficient access to quality family planning
services.
Health and Nutrition
11.   The crude death rate is around 12 per thousand (1990) having fallen over
40X in the past 25 years. Expectation of life at birth has improved slowly and
reached 55 years for females and 56 years for males in 1990. Infant mortality
is high and was estimated at 103 per thousand for 1990, again showing improvement
over the last 20 years (149 per thousand in 1965), and reaching over 200 per
thousand  in Balochistan.    Perinatal  deaths  are  mainly  due  to delivery
complications, poor maternal health and tetanus; post-neonatal deaths are most
commonly due to diarrhea linked problems, malnutrition and communicable diseases
(see Annex 1, Table 4).
12.   Maternal and Child Health (NCh.   The South Asian pattern of higher
mortality of women persists. Maternal mortality continues to be particularly
high (estimated to be around 600 per 100,000 live births in 1980, with recent
survey estimates for small areas ranging from 400 to 1,700 per 100,000). Causes
are complex and include the interaction of excessive/early/late and frequent
childbearing, delivery complications and post-delivery infections. The situation
See 5    Pakistan - Rapid Population Growth in Pakistan, Concerns and Consequences, March 17, 1989
(Report No. 7522-PAX) for a detailed discussion of population related issues.



- 4 -
faced by women is characterized by high levels of anemia and malnutrition; little
or no prenatal care; unsupervised home deliveries; no post-partum care; late, if
any, transfer to higher levels of health care, and severe socio-cultural
constraints.   The overall result is high maternal and perinatal mortality,
especially in Balochistan.
13.   Child mortality and morbidity are dominated by infectious diseases.
Malaria and malnutrition are important contributory factors (see Annex 1, Table
3). The immunization program has had some success. Gastrointestinal infections
are the most important morbidity cause in children; about one-third of children
under five years are estimated to suffer a diarrhea episode during the previous
month.   Use of oral rehydration salts has increased to about 46X of child
diarrhoea cases (1991).
14.   Nutrition5/.  Despite general availability of food, malnutrition is a
major problem particularly for children and pregnant/lactating women. There has
been little improvement during the past decade. Fifty-one percent of children
under-5 are malnourished (low weight for age), 421 show stunting (low height for
age) and 651 suffer from anemia, while 34% of pregnant/lactating women are
underweight for height and 451 are anemic6/. Low birth weights are common (25%
of all births). Only some 30% of children in Balochistan are not malnourished.
Mal-distribution of food between regions, income groups and among family members
contribute to malnutrition, as does poor infant feeding practices and inadequate
or delayed introduction of supplementary food. Iron deficiency is widespread;
vitamin A deficiency is believed to be a serious problem; and iodine deficiency
disorders are prevalent in northern Pakistan.
D. Sector and Proaram Issues and Constraints
Program and Policy Develo DMnt
15.   Health.  Traditionally, resources and priority were given to hospital,
medical school and curative care development while primary and preventive
services were neglected.  A greater focus on basic (rural) health services,
especially infrastructure, developed in the mid-1980s; about 95% of Union
Council7/ areas now have a health facility rising to near 100% in the Punjab.
Allocations for preventive programs have increased from very low levels but the
priority given to preventive services, outreach and health education remains
limited.   Health manpower policy has typically focused on doctors while
development of nursing and paramedical cadres, especially for female staff, has
lagged behind.
16.   Health policy has evolved largely through the five year planning process.
The Seventh Plan (1988-93) gives priority to basic health services, especially
For further discussion, *oo Chapter 2 in op cit World Bank, 1992 (Report No. 10391-PAK).
A/ National nutrition Survey 1985-87, Pekistan National Institute of Health, 1988.
/ The mallost local goverrnnt unit, whicb covcrc a population of 10-20,000.



primary care quality, the immunization program, increased community involvement,
nutrition, staff development and management improvement. A new health policy,
which emphasized the importance of meeting the health needs of the poor and
giving priority to developing an effective primary care system, was published in
May 19908/. It focussed on decentralization of the health administration, the
introduction of community level workers, a major expansion of female paramedical
and nursing staff, encouragement for the private health sector, and improved
financing of the health sector. Drafts of the Eighth Plan (1993-98) emphasize
consolidation of these strategies with an increased focus on MCH.   As a major
initiative for the social sectors, which includes primary health and population
as key areas, the government is preparing a Social Action Programme (SAP)9/,
designed to remove policy barriers and to improve project and program
implementation. The content of the Eighth Plan and the SAP confirm government
statements that no substantial changes in primary health care policy are likely.
17.   Service development has not kept pace with policy and, despite some
successes, important parts of recent primary health care initiatives remain
largely unimplemented. This is partly due to the continuation of a system with
a strong curative focus and which is resistant to change, coupled with
operational difficulties such as management problems and staffing inadequacies.
The implementation of major initiatives will now be through the SAP and the
Eighth Plan. Programs will focus on improving the functioning of the existing
extensive network of rural health facilities through expanded staff pre- and in-
service training, equipment and transport upgrading, improved referral services,
especially for high risk mothers and critically ill children, and introducing
community level workers to strengthen rural outreach. Other key areas include
improved slum primary health services, enhanced management and financing, and
encouragement for the private sector.
18.  fPogiltion.   Population policy has been notable for its changes in
direction.  Between 1965 and 1973 the program relied basically on the use of
traditional  midwives   (daA)   to  motivate   the  population,   distribute
contraceptives, and refer clients to clinics for IUD insertion and sterilization.
Between 1973 and 1977 a Motivation System utilizing well trained couples for
motivation replaced the 9&U. The program was practically non-existent between
1977 and 1980, when it was transferred from the Ministry of Health and Social
Affairs to the Ministry of Planning and Development with an infrastructure
completely separated from health. In 1990, the Ministry of Population Welfare
(MPW) was created to be responsible for population matters. MPW has its own
clinics and service delivery system which are largely independent of government
health facilities. The approach is multi-sectoral. Core programs include family
planning service delivery, including sterilization services, population
information, clinical and non-clinical staff training, contraceptive and supplies
logistics, while supporting programs include those with other government
departments, non-governmental organizations (NGOs) and the contraceptive social
marketing program. Ambitious demographic targets have been set but not met.
/ National Health Policy. Ministry of Health, GoverrAent of Pakistan, 1900.
V Social Action Progrme, Plining Cission, GovenrrAnt of Pakistan, April 1992.



-6-
Government health facilities do not generally offer family planning services10'
despite government policy being explicit, since at least 1985, that all
government health facilities should offer such services. The 1990 health policy,
the SAP and the Eighth Plan reinforce the importance of rectifying this omission.
During 1991, the government took steps to revitalize the population program.
These included: (a) increased political support publicly expressed,,/; and (b)
approval of an "accelerated program' which aims to expand service delivery and
improve efficiency by providing family planning services through health
facilities; expanding the number of fixed and mobile sterilization units;
enhancing and better targeting population education programs; strengthening field
supervision; and enhancing community involvement.
19.   Since 1980, basic population policy has been largely unchanged.   The
current multi-sectoral approach is the one most likely to be effective. However,
as for health policy, the implementation of population policy has not matched the
plans. Contraceptive use remains very low and, although there has been some
improvement, little priority is still given to the program by most politicians
and administrators.
20.   Nutrition.   Nutrition policy has been fragmentary, implementation has
received low priority and it has commonly been equated only with supplementary
feeding. Subsidies on selected foods have not been targeted on poor households.
Federal institutional capacity to address nutrition problems has grown over the
past six years, but provincial capacity remains very weak.  The 1990 health
policy proposed to: introduce applied nutrition into general education, employ
nutrition specialists in the public health system, integrate nutrition into
maternal and child health care, and target food subsidies. The draft Eighth Plan
and SAP reinforce this approach. The major programs planned are in nutrition
policy development, micronutrient deficiency control, nutrition surveillance and
supplementary feeding, and mass nutrition education.
Organization and Kanaaeenot
21.   The Public Sector.   The Ministry of Health12/ (MOH) of the federal
government is responsible for health policy making and coordination.
Responsibility for health service provision mainly lies with the provincial
health departments each headed by a Secretary, assisted by a Director General (or
Director) of Health Services (DHS).  District officials report to the DHS and
include the District Health Officer (DHO) and the District Hospital Medical
Superintendent. Officers in charge of lower level government facilities and the
district managers of vertical programs report through the DHO. Further details
are given in Annex 6.  Organization diagrams are given in Charts LA to 1C.
Q The exception is tho reproductive health (i. . sterilization) proarm  which largely functions from
governraet hospitals.
W For x plo, mee the Prine Minister's address to the National Population Conference, July 1991.
U The Ministry's full title iL the Ministry of Health, Special ducation and Social Welfare.



Municipal authorities provide some health services but their nature varies
widely.
22.   There are three main layers in the government health system:  (a) Basic
Health Units (BHU) serving populations of 10-20,000 designed to offer simple
curative and preventive, including maternal and child, care; (b) Rural Health
Centres (RHC) providing more sophisticated curative care, including inpatient
services, and preventive care for 60-100,000 people; and (c) a network of
tehsill31 hospitals (typically one per tehsil with an average 40-60 beds)
backed-up by district hospitals (usually one in each district with an average 150
beds) offering first line referral services, with tertiary care offered by 58
specialized and teaching hospitals located in the larger urban areas. Alongside
this system, there are vertical programs in population (see below), malaria,
tuberculosis, supplementary feeding, immunization, oral rehydration and Ali
training, each with its own management.
23.   Public health management training has yet to be established; management
skills are learnt 'on-the-job' and performance is very variable.   A federal
Health Services Academy for management development has been created but is not
functional. The main needs are at the provincial level and the plans are to
strengthen provincial and district management teams, introduce management
training and improve supervisory practices.
24.   Management constraints include:  (a) centralized decision making separated
from service operational responsibility; (b) complex structures resulting from
not consolidating existing and new schemes; (c) the lack of integration between
the various vertical programs and the basic health services; (d) a generally poor
or wrongly focussed reward system including the virtual absence of incentives for
working in rural facilities; (e) at the village level, very limited outreach from
health facilities, and no organized public sector involvement with communities
or link with the daJ; and (f) no attempt to link budget allocations and
performance or efficiency.
25.   The organization of the population program is different to health; federal
control of the program remains considerable. The program has its own structure
of federal, provincial and district officials reporting to the Secretary of
Population Welfare and is the most separate of the vertical programs. Their own
service delivery system, which covers a much smaller proportion of the country
than the general health system, includes 1250 Family Welfare Centres (FWC), about
135 Reproductive Health Centres (located in government hospitals and offering
contraceptive surgery and other family planning methods) and training
institutions. This separation inevitably leads to inefficiencies in both systems
and there is potential for improved coverage or savings from closer coordination
or by  combining  the  two.    The  population  system  suffers  from  similar
organizational problems as the health system but they are less structural and
V An adinistrative unit below the district l1el, covering sce 300,000 people on average.



more managerial, arising from the unique federal/provincial administrative
arrangements.14/
26.   Effective health services require efficient support systems, especially in
the areas of medicines and supplies, laboratory services, transport and
communications, and equipment maintenance and repair. Such systems have often
been neglected. Provincial procurement of medicines and suDlIles is centralized
with each province having its own central warehouse and distribution system.
Pharmaceutical budgets are insufficient, inappropriate medicines are purchased
and there are frequent shortages. The provincial governments plan to introduce
essential drugs programs. In addition to drug rationalization, this will include
decentralization of logistics, staff training and transport strengthening.
Laboratory services are typically deficient in providing timely results of an
acceptable standard. To alleviate these difficulties, basic laboratory services
at rural health facilities will be established or strengthened, supplies improved
and existing staff trained. Improved transDort and communications is essential
for better emergency handling, service provision and management.  Government
plans include purchase of replacement and additional vehicles and establishing
telephone or radio links where feasible.   Poor maintenance and repair of
eguipment is a major factor in inefficient operation especially of larger health
facilities. The government has a continuing program to establish a network of
repair workshops, increase standardization of equipment and train technicians.
Further details on these initiatives are given in Chapter II.
27.   The Private Sector.  It is unusually large and accounts for about 60X of
total health expenditure. It can be divided into three parts: the modern, the
traditional and the NGO.  The mode   consists principally of general medical
practitioners. There are about 12,000 private practitioners, in addition to some
26,000 government doctors who commonly have their own private practices. Private
hospitals number about 520 in addition to over 300 maternity and nursing homes,
most with less than 50 beds.   There are over 20,000 private clinics and
dispensaries, mostly simple general practitioners offices concentrated in urban
areas. The private sector is particularly small in Balochistan. For background
figures see Annex 1, Tables 7 and 8.  Pakistan has a large pharmaceutical
industry with 64 manufacturers; small chemists shops, estimated at 11,000, are
common, concentrated in urban areas, and supply most medicines. Consumption of
pharmaceuticals is unusually high (2.21 of average household expenditure in
1985).   Pakistan manufactures and exports significant quantities of simple
medical equipment and instruments; medical equipment repair capabilities for more
complex equipment are very limited.
28.   Issues related to the private commercial health sector include:  (a) the
widely accepted need to introduce a feasible regulatory framework to ensure
minimum clinical and ethical standards; (b) how to involve the private sector in
preventive services; (c) as private services are concentrated in urban areas and
serve the better off, how can the government save resources and increase its
concentration on the less privileged; (d) poor links between the private and
/ For further discussion an orsaniatiam and * nagsnmt in the public scator health and population
services, sea chaptor 3 in op cit World Bank, 1982 (Report No. 10351-PAK).



- 9 -
public sectors; and (e) little development of alternative forms of health
financing such as health insurance.151
29.   The traditional comprises dais (i.e. traditional birth attendants) and
traditional healers. Dai are found in rural and urban areas and are thought to
number over 100,000.   They attend the great majority of births and are the
overwhelming first choice for prenatal care by pregnant women (681).  Three
different systems of traditional healing (tibb- i-unani, ayurvedic and homeopathy)
are practiced and, in all, there are about 54,000 healers of which some 15X are
formally qualified.   The government is expanding its well-established {al
training program and plans to increase past modest support for traditional
practitioner training.
30.   Although there are over 8,000 NG0s in Pakistan nearly three-quarters of
which are in urban areas, mostly with a social welfare orientation, they are
often small, locally-oriented and usually are neither financially nor technically
self-sufficient; as many as two-thirds of them are thought to be dormant. In
contrast there are a very few large, effective and well-run local NGOs working
in the health and population sector, mostly based in the Punjab. Overall, NGO
contributions to the health sector are modest and markedly less than that of NGOs
in neighboring countries.
Service Provision
31.   The performance of the primaar  health facilities has not kept pace with
their rapid expansion; service quality and utilization are low due to systemic
problems of manpower, management, finance, staff attitudes and the social setting
(see Annex 1, Table 13). Outreach from facilities is minimal and preventive
services operate as vertical rather than integrated programs. Services for rural
women face these general problems and other issues specific to women, which are
particularly severe in Balochistan, include: (a) the lack of the integration of
the _4i with the health facility; (b) inadequate female staffing hindering access
to care; (c) an ineffective referral system; (d) lack of health education; (e)
limited accessibility of family planning advice and services; and (f) constraints
on female mobility.   There is potential to reduce substantially infant and
maternal mortality during the next five years and the government's program will
focus on improved MCH services, strengthened referral systems, expanded female
paramedical training, increased "I training,le/ experiments with sustainable
community health worker schemes, the development of strong health education
programs (an area which has been very neglected) and emphasis on the nutrition
of pregnant and lactating mothers.
32.   Public hosRita1 outpatient facilities are frequently grossly overloaded,
particularly in the larger hospitals, due to the absence of an effective referral

system and the poor quality care offered at lower level facilities. Inpatient
facilities at the teaching hospitals consistently run at around 80-100 occupancy
while district hospitals usually have rates of about 701.   In the smaller
hospitals, especially at the tehsil level, occupancy rates of 501 or less are
common with rates dropping to 25-30X in RHCs.   Communications and emergency
services between the hospitals and lower level facilities are rudimentary,
especially in Balochistan. Management problems throughout the hospital sector
are severe.17/
33.   The large private health sector is growing, especially in urban areas,
Partly driven by the continuing over-supply of medical graduates. The government
has taken some limited steps to encourage the growth of the private sector
including exemptions from duties for imported medical equipment and establishing
credit sources.  There is no effective regulation on the quality and mix of
services offered by private providers. Experiments with financing mechanisms,
including health insurance, are beginning. Until recently, little government
encouragement was given to the development of NGOs in the health sector. During
1991, three provinces and the federal government established 'Health
Foundations', a major aim of which is to stimulate NGO health sector work"1
often through matching grants. None of these Foundations are yet operational.
MNW has promoted population through the private sector by: (a) providing
incentives to general practitioners to offer family planning services; (b)
marketing of condoms through private commercial channels; and (c) establishing
a fund to stimulate NGO population work.
Staffing and Training
34.   Nationally, there is about one doctor for every 2,700 people, one nurse for
every 7.8 hospital beds and one paramedical for every 1,545 people. Background
information on the main health cadres is given in Annex 1 (Tables 5, 6 and 8) and
Annex 2. With the exception of the doctor/population ratio, these ratios are
among the worst in Asia. Most female staff are in short supply, with Balochistan
facing acute problems. The rapid expansion of medical training, moving from 5
to 17 medical schools over the past 20 years, with little increase in nursing and
support staff training has led to very unbalanced staffing and an estimated 6,000
currently under-employed young doctors. Health manpower planning in Pakistan has
been ad hoc and for short time horizons. However, a health manpower plan for all
formally trained staff was completed in August 199019/ and implementation is
beginning. Priority will be given to addressing the chronic shortages of female
paramedical staff who are essential for providing MCH services. The program will
expand paramedical training capacity; improve training quality; strengthen in-
service training; enhance career structures, social status and rural service

(Roport No. 10391-PAh).

conditions;  and establish manpower planning capacity.   The plans call for
substantial increases in nursing and paramedical cadres (by 1501 to over 1,000X
according to cadre) and a reduction of about 251 in the medical graduate output.
The latter recommendation has yet to be accepted, although the Punjab recently
reduced somewhat the number of medical training places.  Until decisions are
taken, resources will continue to be unnecessarily diverted from higher priority
areas.   These problems are largely a reflection of wider national issues
concerning the training of highly qualified manpower.
Expenditure and Financin4
35.   Since the early 1970s, total health and population public expenditure
(development and recurrent) increased slowly in real terms, peaking at Rs. 2,734
million (constant 1972/73 prices) or 1.08X of GNP in 1987/88, and subsequently
declining to Rs. 2,243 million or 0.73X of GNP in 1991/92 (see Annex 1, Table 9).
Overall, this represented a low 2.8X of total public expenditure in 1991/92.
When supplemented with the proportionately larger private sector expenditure,
estimated in 1987 at Rs. 4,608 million (1972/73 prices) or 2.42 1 of GNP,20/
aggregate expenditure on a per capita basis is broadly similar to comparable
countries,  yet health  indicators  are considerably worse.    Besides  these
inadequacies, public spending on health is inequitably distributed and
inefficient.
36.   In the 1960s and 1970s, very high proportions of public investment in the
health sector were spent on urban hospitals and medical training with less than
101  spent  on  rural  health.    Following  the  start  of  the  rural  health
infrastructure construction program in the mid-1980s, the proportion of the
investment budget spent on rural health, preventive and nutrition programs has
risen markedly to about 55-60X of the total (see Annex 1, Table 10). As the
construction program is completed, this proportion is gradually reducing.
However, as this was not matched by adequate increases in recurrent spending,
this reorientation has had limited impact. The present level of the recurrent
budgets is barely adequate to cover operating costs in all provinces and it is
still biased toward hospitals leaving only 25-40X for rural health (see Annex 1,
Table 11). Salaries take an increasing proportion (at least 551), and as much
as 70X in one province, leaving inadequate amounts for medicines and other
running costs.
37.   The financing of the sector raises a number of issues.  First, additional
resources are needed to address critical health needs and to bring levels of
spending to internationally comparable standards. Total resources in the health
sector seem to have stagnated and under-investment in the public sector
continues. Recent increases in health allocations by the provincial governments
have been off-set by general salary increases as well as diminished federal
government health spending. Second, substantial recurrent budget increases and
compositional shifts in spending are required to finance projected staff
increases as well as to sustain rural health infrastructure and services. Third,
QHoalth Sector in Pakistan: A financing cnd hZpenditure Study, Ministry of Planning *nd Developmnt,
confidential final report, April 1988.



- 12 -
considerable variations in unit costs in similar facilities suggest potential for
substantial gains in expenditure efficiency and effectiveness.   Fourth, the
growing financing needs of the sector require greater private sector
participation and improved cost recovery, especially in public hospitals. The
lack of cost recovery in the public sector is important since only some 4X of
recurrent hospital expenditure is recovered mainly through very low user fees.
The governments intend to raise fees and other charges, especially for tertiary
facilities, but wish to avoid adverse public health consequences. There are no
plans to increase the present nominal fees at primary care facilities. These
policy developments are supported by IDA and are being incorporated in the Social
Action Programe (SAP).
38.   A start has been made recently in addressing some of these crucial issues.
Real per capita expenditure levels for the sector are being protected under an
on-going adjustment program. The SAP is adopting a basic strategy of increasing
resources to the social sectors through improved public expenditure programuing
as well as restructuring inter- and intra-sectoral priorities in financing. The
Eighth Plan, under preparation, will also address these issues.   Tentative
allocations for the nation,l health development program amount to Rs. 25 billion,
a 211 real increase over the Seventh Plan allocation. About half of this amount
is proposed for primary health and preventive programs.
E. Sector DeveloRunt Strate&X
39.   Government Strategy. The government's development strategy for the health
sector can be synthesized from the Seventh Plan, the 1990 health policy, and the
national and provincial preparatory work for the Eighth Plan and the SAP as:
(a) health services becoming more effective, efficient, affordable and
acceptable; (b) greater priority for disease prevention, health promotion and
MCH; (c) better balance between urban and rural services; and (d) improved
comiunity participation.   It will focus on consolidating past investments,
preventive and curative service availability and quality, decentralization,
improving female staff availability, promoting the private sector, improving
support and referral systems, mobilizing additional financial resources, and
encouraging the traditional medical systems. For R02ulation, the government is
retaining the same multi-sectoral strategy but focusing particularly on: (a)
improving the coverage of family planning especially by providing services at all
health outlets; and (b) improving the efficiency of the program.
40.   IDA's Strate&X and Role.   The successful implementation of the social
sector aspects of macroeconomic policy and adjustment operations (see para. 42
below) and of the SAP are key parts of IDA's strategy to address systemic issues
in the social sectors. These are designed to both protect and improve sector
financing and to address chronic issues many of which are cross-sectoral. Within
this framework, the approach is to help the government to implement a coherent,
affordable and technically sound health and population program. The priorities
are to: (a) improve the system's quality; (b) improve the internal efficiency
and cost-effectiveness of the existing system; (c) strengthen the institutional
capability of the system; and (d) provide the required human, management, and
financial resources. The focus will therefore be on improving the utilization



- 13 -
of existing facilities, on the development of the quality and quantity of human
resources, and on the capability of the system to provide and finance the
necessary services. Particular attention will be given to the needs of women,
and the rural and urban poor.   The needs in the sector are enormous but
absorptive constraints are severe. IDA's approach is to address wider sectoral
problems through various adjustment and policy operations typically implemented
over two to three years, while sector investment operations both support this
approach and address more technical and capacity building constraints over a
longer implementation period. This is an early stage of IDA's involvement in the
sector and, given satisfactory performance, follow-up projects could be
anticipated.
41.   External SuDRort.  Foreign assistance for the health and population sector
has been significant but generally less than that received by some nearby
countries.   For population, support has been substantial (652) with USAID
traditionally being the largest financier; UNFPA, ODA and IDA have also made
significant contributions. Foreign assistance has played a much smaller role in
the health program. Again, USAID has usually been the largest single donor with
UNICEF, CIDA, JICA, ADB and IDA assisting substantially2l/.   If the expected
withdrawal from Pakistan in 1993 of the entire USAID program is realized, there
will be a substantial fall in the external support provided to the sector. Donor
coordination operates mainly at a local informal level.
42.   Lessons Learned.   IDA financed one previous health sector project.22/
As it is at a very early stage, there is little implementation experience to draw
upon.  Since the approved and proposed projects are similar, the experience
gained during preparation of the earlier project was drawn upon. A population
project was completed in December 198923/.  It  achieved only limited success
due to: (a) the erosion of political support for the population program; (b) an
under-estimation of the institutional constraints on implementation; and (c)
difficulties arising from the unusual distribution of program responsibilities
between the federal and provincial levels. IDA experience in education projects
and other donor experience in the health sector identified problems of:  (a)
implementation arising from national projects involving multiple provinces; (b)
lack of integration of project management with mainstream government
administration; (c) delayed project start due to difficulties in preparation and
approval of government project documentation; (d) appointment of project staff;
and (e) the need for sustained external technical support. These lessons have
been taken into account in the proposed project, through:  (a) limiting the
numbers of provinces involved; (b) seeking to establish more flexible
/ The major donors to pri-mry health are: ADD (rural health infrastructure, equipment maintenanoc,

population, malaria), and IDA (maternal health, in-service training, management).

implementation mechanisms; (c) posting project coordination staff and obtaining
government project approvals as early as possible; (d) working through the
existing implementation systems; and (e) simplifying project design as far as
possible but consistent with maintaining technical standards.
II. THE PROJECT
A.  Provincial Programs
Programs Setting
43.   The proposed project, the Second Family Health Project (FHII), will support
the health development programs in the provinces of Balochistan and the Punjab.
While both provinces follow the national patterns of sector problems, programs
and issues as discussed in the preceding chapter, there are also significant
differences. In Balochistan (estimated total population 5.7 million in 1990),
health conditions are the worst and the services are the least developed in the
country. This is partly due to the low population density and rugged terrain
combined with  a very  traditional  society.    Female  health  problems  are
particularly acute with maternal and infant mortality estimated to be
exceptionally high. The lack of trained staff, especially of females, is the
main constraint to expanding services.
44. In contrast, the Puniab (estimated total population 60.9 million in 1991)
is the wealthiest, the most densely populated and the most urbanized province,
with the largest and best educated population and the most developed health
services.   Health and social development indicators are around the national
average but are significantly poorer in rural and urban slum areas, and much
remains to be done. Immunization and diarrheal disease control programs have
made significant progress although communicable and infectious diseases continue
to be serious problems. The rural health infrastructure is largely complete but
staffing problems, especially of female paramedical staff, continue. Annex 1
(Tables 1, 2 and 12) present background information on the health sector in
Balochistan and the Punjab.
45.   The provincial governments are preparing strategies designed to improve
basic social services through provincial SAPs for the next two financial years
(1993/94 to 1994/95).  These SAPs will address major issues and constraints,
especially in implementation, in the sectors. Within this framework, sectoral
investment plans, or programs, for the next five financial years (1993/94 to
1997/98), are also being prepared. These programs will be included in the new
Eighth Five-Year Development Plan for each province (see Annex 1, Table 14).
Obiectives and Priorities
46. The main objective of these programs is to improve the health status of the
population in general, but with special emphasis on high risk and underprivileged
sections of the community.   To accomplish this objective, these programs are
adopting measures to improve the provision of basic health services.



- 15 -
47.   Both the provinces and the federal government are committed to improving
basic health services. They have confirmed the commitment made in the Policy
Framework PaRer (PFP) to increase development and recurrent spending in the
health sector, with additional resources to be made available for primary health
and especially maternal and child health care. These commitments are reinforced
in the proposed Public Sector Adjustment Loan (PSAL) where the provinces are also
committed to address policy issues in such areas as family planning services, the
distribution of resources within health budgets, and the adoption of user charges
in large hospitals. In the provincial Social Action Programs (SAP), on which
work is continuing, detailed time-bound plans are being developed for the
realization of these commitments over the coming two years as well as for steps
to improve the implementation of ongoing activities. These progressively more
detailed and increasingly provincially orientated measures provide the framework
for the provincial health development programs.
48.   Since  substantial  investments had been made by  the governments  in
developing health infrastructure, a central theme of the provincial health
development orograms is to concentrate on investments which will fully
operationalise the existing facilities (such as staffing, supervision and support
services) rather than on those which will expand the network.   This will be
linked with the required administrative, managerial and technical changes in
order to ensure that these improvements are implemented. Both the Balochistan
and the Punjab provincial programs follow this approach and are similar, albeit
differing in detail.
Programs Description
49.   The Balochistan and the Punjab health programs  include  a range of
expenditures and measures throughout the two health systems. The development
expenditures cover: (a) primary services; (b) secondary and tertiary services;
and (c) service support such as training and management, combined with the
provision of an adequate recurrent budget. These expenditures will be enabled
by key policy reform measures and by strengthened planning, implementation and
monitoring. Within this framework, the Balochistan program focuses on putting
the package of essential basic service elements in place and especially on those
aspects which are most deficient: staffing, especially of female paramedicals,
transport and referral systems, upgrading of the health facility infrastructure
to minimum standards, and improving supervision and management.   The Punjab
program differs somewhat since the essential basic elements for service provision
are generally available; priority areas therefore include improved village
outreach, better availability of supplies, enhanced training, strengthened
management, and controlled hospital spending.
50.   In primary care, the programs will focus on rural women and children.  The
programs aim to increase accs  to health services, strengthen service quality,
and improve service utilization.  The past investment in health facilities will
be better used, with the small numbers of proposed new facilities in the program
being mainly to accommodate rapidly increasing populations. The programs include
a number of inputs in other areas intended to improve rural primary care
including communicable disease control programs.   The provincial programs also



- 16 -
include provision for experiments in primary health care services for slum
dwellers and the subsequent introduction of major urban activities.
51.   In secondary and tertiary care, an important part of the provincial
strategies is to limit additional hospital investment, and to divert the
resources saved towards primary services. Thus, program inputs will include only
limited new hospital construction or expansion; equipment investments largely
confined to those which improve efficiency; and the strengthening of hospital
management. Some of the program inputs included under primary care will also
directly benefit hospitals, especially for support services.
52.   Support areas. To support these service inputs, particularly for primary
care, the programs propose to improve staft capabilities and performance and to
increase the availability of staff (especially female paramedical staff) through
in-service training, establishing new and expanding existing training schools,
and improving trainee recruitment and staff retention.          nagement will be
developed through management training, developing monitoring and information
systems, and strengthening supervision. The programs also include experiments
in health care financing, efforts to increase the production of pharmaceutical
and medical equipment, and strategies for the development of the private health
sector; these will not be directly supported through the project.
53.   In addition, there is a federal program, part of which supports provincial
activities directly, such as for communicable disease control, while the balance
is for national activities with some limited provincial involvement, for example
in health research.
Policy Actions
54. The provincial governments' commitment to improving and better funding
primary health care is reflected in the comitments given in the PFP, the PSAL
and finally in the preparation of the SAP. Under the SAP, the government has
committed itself to the implementation of an eleven point program to improve the
provision of primary education, primary health, family planning and rural water
supply and sanitation. In relation to health, these points include improving
conditions of employment in rural areas, providing family planning in all health
outlets, increasing budgetary allocations, improving the data base to monitor
outcomes of services, prioritizing programs and projects, improving resource
availability, and increasing decentralization (see Annex 3 for details).
Continuing work on the SAP has the objective of ensuring the removal of
constraints to implementation, ensuring adequate recurrent expenditures and
ensuring that new investments are made according to additional implementation
capacity.
B.  Proiect Objectives. Suary DescriRtion and Policy Actions
Proiect Obiectives and Priorities
55.   The primary objective of the project is to improve the health status of the
population of Balochistan and the Punjab through assisting the provincial
governments with the implementation of their respective provincial health



- 17 -
development programs, including strengthening institutional capacity, over the
next five years. Within this, priority is on the primary health aspects of the
programs including assisting with: (a) a shift of emphasis towards primary health
care and away from secondary and tertiary care; and (b) a shift of emphasis away
from curative care and towards preventive and promotive services.   Primary
service priority will be established through the more equitable distribution of
resources such as drugs and consumable supplies, through staff posting
(especially for female paramedical staff), and through financial allocations.
Moreover, savings achieved through improved efficiency in the sector will be
invested in primary health services. Within these broad objectives, the project
will support the key areas within the governments' programs of: (a) enhancing
the provision of maternal and child health care; and (b) facilitating these
changes, particularly through strengthened training and management.
56.   Because the needs greatly exceed the available human, implementation and
financial resources, difficult choices had to be made in the formulation of the
provincial programs.   Within the resulting priorities,  the conclusions of
workshops in Balochistan and the Punjab determined the priority areas of the
program for potential IDA and cooperating donors (KfW and ODA working in close
consultation with the other donors) support.   Thus,  the proposed project
comprises a group of priority sub-programs of the total development program. It
also follows from the 1991 Family Health project which focused on the North-West
Frontier Province (NWFP) and Sindh.
57.   The proposed project will support the implementation of the provincial
programs  through:    (a)  financing  investments  in critical  areas  of  the
governments' plan for primary health services development with priority given to
maternal and child health; (b) supporting institutional strengthening in the
health sector which will improve both service quality and the efficiency of
service delivery in critically deficient areas; and (c) facilitating the
implementation of Rolicy reforms in the sector which will enable the above
measures to be realized. Work on preparing the government health programs is
continuing, but the primary care aspects are well advanced. The latter is being
supported by several bilateral donors and international agencies.   During
Negotiations, draft health chapters of the Eighth Plan for the two provinces were
discussed with IDA.   Assurances were obtained during Negotiations that an
official statement of health sector policy, strategy and priorities vill be
prepared for the two provinces, discussed with IDA and issued by December
1993.

to be high priority. It comprises the following three major components:

iii. developing monitoring, information and financial systems; and
iv.   strengthening supervisory skills and practices;
Additionally, limited Federal inputs include: (a) developing primary care
activities, including health services and management development, in the
Islamabad Capital Territory (ICT) including supporting one NGO project; and (b)
strengthening health systems research capacity.
Policy Actions
59.   The provincial governments' commitment to improving and better funding of
primary health care is reflected in the PFP, the PSAL, the SAPs (see Annex 3 for
details) and the sector development programs. The policy actions agreed through
the project focus on a limited number of key aspects which are crucial
operationally to improving the efficiency and effectiveness of primary health
care and maternal and child health services. In summary they are:
Actions to imro've primary care services:
a. introduction of essential drugs lists (see para. 73);
b. improved coordination among the communicable disease control programs
(see para. 75); and
c. implementation of policies to provide family planning services in
government health facilities (see para. 77);



- 20 -
Actions to improve suRRort services:
a. introduction of a systematic in-service staff training program (see
para. 87);
b. review and improvement of service conditions for female paramedical
staff (see para. 91);
c. introduction of a management training program (see para. 95); and
d. increased delegation of powers to district officers (see para. 100).
In addition, the project includes agreements to ensure the availability of
sufficient additional recurrent funds for basic health services (see para. 60).
Annual Reviews
60.   The annual program reviews between the governments, IDA and the cooperating
donors will be an essential element in improving the management of the sector.
The focus will be on the planning and monitoring of the implementation of the
program. In these reviews, progress in implementing the program will be assessed
and work-plans and program targets developed for the coming year, which will
conform both with the agreed overall sectoral priority investments and the agreed
policy actions. Monitoring indicators for these annual reviews are specified in
Annex 3. Assurances were obtained during Negotiations that, by March 31 of each
year each province, with IDA and the cooperating donors, will: (a) review the
program and project implementation for the past fiscal year; (b) review and agree
the schedules and planned development budgetary allocations for the program and
project implementation for the coming fiscal year; and (c) agree planned overall
recurrent budgetary allocations which will include this IDA project and other
assistance for the coming fiscal year. These agreements will be reflected in the
budget proposal for each fiscal year. Assurances were also obtained that each
province will provide sufficient development and recurrent funding to primary and
rural health services throughout the program; that the recurrent health budget
proposal will reflect the real rate of growth of GDP; and that preference for the
allocation of additional recurrent resources will be given to basic health
services and non-salary items. The federal government, IDA and the cooperating
donors will also review annually the funds required for carrying out both the
provincial programs and the federal activities.
C. Detailed Project DescriRtion
61.   This section presents in detail the project components.  These components
are : (a) Strengthening Primary Health Services; (b) Staff Development; and (c)
Management and Organizational Development. To facilitate comparisons with the
development programs of Balochistan and the Punjab, the section is divided into
three categories: (a) primary health services, subdivided into inputs to improve
service (i) access, (ii) quality, and (iii) utilization; (b) secondary and
tertiary health services; and (c) support services, subdivided into (i) staff
development and (ii) management, with more details given for sub-programs



- 21 -
directly affecting maternal and child care and those proposed for FHII financing.
A sumary implementation chart is given at Annex 10.24/
Strenutheninn  Primary Health Services  (total project contribution US$64.5
million, of which Balochistan US$12.9 million and the Punjab US$51.6 million,
including contingencies)
62.   This series of sub-programs aims at strengthening primary health services
in two priority areas by introducing an enhanced package of maternal health
services including family planning (also known as 'safe motherhood') and by
improving the coverage and effectiveness of communicable disease control
activities. The additional services to be provided will supplement or strengthen
existing services while some existing services will be reorganized. In summary
(details are given in Annex 4), the services affected will be:
a. at the village level, where few services presently exist, expanding and
introducing prenatal care (including high risk pregnancy screening),
supervised delivery, postnatal care (including family planning and
nutrition) through trained TBAs and female CHWs (CHWs only in the
Punjab); introducing minor curative care, communicable disease case
finding, and health and hygiene promotion mainly through CHWs;
b. at the BHU level, ensuring that present curative and preventive
services function and especially strengthening prenatal, supervised
delivery and postnatal services, and adding links to the TBAs/CHWs and
to referral levels; introducing family planning; strengthening
diagnostic services; reorganizing tuberculosis and malaria control
services; and enhancing health education and nutrition activities;
c. at the RH level, as for the maternal health services at BHUs but with
the capability to handle higher risk cases, and also with a greater
focus on the preventive and promotive services; and
d. at the tehsil hosRital level, focusing on improved emergency and
referral services, with some enhancement to district hosRital maternal
diagnostic services especially in Balochistan.

64.   Koanding TRA trainin  and eg2erimentina with and introducing coumumitv
health workers.   This sub-program will comprise the expansion of the well-
established and continuing, mainly UNICEF financed, TBA training program, create
a link between the trained TBAs and the local primary health care facility, and
start (in Balochistan) and strengthen (in the Punjab), in-service training.25/
Following a detailed evaluation, adjustments in the TEA training program are
being implemented through phasing-in the involvement of local BHU/RHC staff and
changes to and expansion of the present TBA training teams. The BHU/RHC will
become responsible for the training and support of TBAs from the villages in its
catchment area. The sub-program anticipates continuing UNICEF financing, as well
as additional support to be provided through FHII, which together will meet
almost all of the needs.  The project financed incremental activities in both
provinces will include about 4,800 TBAs to be trained initially, 2,000 to receive
refresher training and the provision of about 14,000 kits and their resupply plus
supporting simple teaching materials.
65. National policy is to introduce a new cadre of community health workers.
At present, there is little outreach work from health facilities and the
introduction of community health workers will help fill this gap. In the Punjab,
the program plans to start with an experimental approach by developing and
implementing their training initially in one district.  Different modalities for
paying  these  workers  will  be  tried.    Assuming  that  the  experience  is
satisfactory, the training will be expanded to some 500 CHWs initially.  The
project will support this entire sub-program.   Despite national policy, the
Balochistan government's view is that CHWs are unlikely to be successful in the
local social and logistical setting.  No inputs are included in the program for
government CHW related activities in Balochistan.
66.   Posting additional staff. Rarticularly female 2aramedical staff.  Many
existing rural facilities, especially in Balochistan, are unable to offer health
services for women due to lack of female paramedical staff.  In the Puniab,
almost all positions have been created and funded, but many are vacant; in
Balochistan, there is a need to create additional positions to supplement
existing vacant posts so that the small number of female paramedical staff
expected to become available will be employed.   The program, with project
financing, will assist with the salaries of only about 90 Lady Health Visitors
and Female Health Technicians, all incremental positions in rural facilities in
Balochistan, which is a small part of the optimal provincial requirement. In
addition, incremental costs of female paramedical staff for improved service
outreach will be provided in both 2rovinces.
67.   Strengthenin  the  referral  system and enhancing  emergency handlinj
ca2acitl.   The rapid identification and proper handling of emergencies is
I Due to the different provincial situations, in Balochistan Thn are initially trained by mobile
teems while in the B&J&j this is done at fixed itecs.

essential; it also helps establish the credibility of the health system.
Logistical and cultural constraints differ markedly between the two provinces.
Although the program will improve the entire hospital system (see below), the
enhancement of the referral system is a priority, especially the identification
and referral of obstetric emergencies through inclusion of risk assessment as a
key item in TBA and CHW training and through establishing a system for early
referral to the BHU/RHC.   Referral support services will be improved by the
programs, with project financing, through installing telephones or, where
necessary, radios for BHUs and RHCs (in the Puniab only), increased funds for
transport hire and the provision of ambulances and funds for experiments in
Balochistan on alternatives for emergency transport and communications.
68.   The programs include support for emergency referral services at almost all
lower level hospitals in both Rrovinces. This will include upgrading existing
emergency facilities including labor rooms, at six divisional hospitals in
Balochistan and at about 30 district hospitals in the Punjab. Additionally,
enhancing the first line referral services available for obstetric emergencies
at key district hospitals and RHCs in Balochistan and at about 260 tehsil
hospitals and RHCs in the Punijab. Physicians, supplementally trained through
special surgical and emergency procedure short courses, will be posted at those
facilities where specialists are not available. In total, over 300 operating
facilities will be partly re-equipped and provided with additional medicines and
supplies. Additionally, in Balochistan, equipment and supplies will be provided
for establishing a labor room at 36 district hospitals and RHCs. The project
will finance this sub-program.
69.   Exoanding NGO health and Rormlation activities.  As discussed in Chapter
I, the policy of the governments is to encourage the expansion of NGO health and
population activities particularly through the nascent Health Foundations. The
provincial budgets include significant allocations for grants-in-aid to NGOs.
The project will support part of this sub-program through some 13 specific sub-
projects to expand the work of five well-established Pakistani NGOs. Generally,
the construction and equipping of facilities, plus associated training and
experimental community health schemes, will be supported with the NGOs being
responsible for almost all incremental recurrent costs. In Balochistan, five
sub-projects with four NGOs have been appraised; in the Puniab, criteria for
identifying suitable NGOs and sub-projects have been agreed which will ensure
that activities are consistent with the overall sub-program objectives. It is
anticipated that facilities to be supported will include four maternity
hospitals, about 85 MCH clinics, equipment for one nurse training school, and
training costs.
