72. Upgrading facilities and egui ment at selected Basic Health Units *nd MC_
Centres and  Drovina the availability of essential medicines *nd supplies
Although the network of rural health facilities is generally adequate,
particularly in the Punjab, the program includes provision for selected upgrading
and expansion of facilities and the construction of a further 8-9X rural
facilities in both provinces (197 BHUs in the Punjab and 50 BHUs and RHCs in
Balochistan). Within this facility development sub-program, the project will
only support priority MCH-related facilities in Balochistan, through upgrading
about 30 Dispensaries to MCH Centres and their equipping and staffing, providing
about 48 residences for female paramedical staff at existing MCH Centres and
providing additional and replacement MCH equipment to about 150 BHUs. In the
Punjab, the project will finance the sub-program activities which will provide
MCH equipment, furniture and other minor equipment to some 2,300 BHUs and MCH
Centres.  Additionally, in both Rrovinces, about 138 temporary (satellite)
clinics, linked to existing facilities, will be established and equipped in areas
where facilities are inaccessible to women due to social constraints.
73.   Supplying additional basic medicines and supplies to some 2,360 BHUs and
MCH Centres from within an approved list of essential items graded to the type
of facility. Such essential drugs lists are in various stages of preparation and
approval by the federal and provincial governments but have yet to be
implemented. In the Punjab, experiments with co-munity contributions to facility
medicine budgets will be included. In both provinces, the distribution system
for medicines and supplies will be reorganized and decentralized through
additional staff, training, studies and improved transport.  Assurances were
obtained during Negotiations that essential drugs lists will be approved by the
Balochistan and Punjab Departments of Health by July 1994 and introduced by
December 1994.
74.   Enhancg  nutrition activities. Nutrition activities typically have been
led from the Federal level while province-led inputs have been weak.  The
provincial nutrition sub-programs are being developed and are likely to include
strengthened nutrition education and micronutrient distribution. IDA is in the
early stages of developing a nutrition project, which will take up major
nutrition-related interventions. This project focuses on inputs which will pave
the way for this proposed nutrition project, and concentrates on aspects which
will have a direct effect on MCH.  Thus the project includes only limited
nutrition activities including increasing the presently very few technical
nutrition staff, developing and updating provincial nutrition strategies, and
integrating nutrition activities and education into MCH Centres, BHUs and RHCs
routine work. Inputs are similar in both Balochistan and the Puniab, although
nutrition problems are more serious in Balochistan.   Provision for basic
equipment, supplies, education materials, transport, studies and consultancies
and incremental technical staff is included.



- 25 -
75.   Strenethenine and e2wandina communicable disease control activities and
diagnostic caRacities. This sub-program covers a wide range of preventive health
activities including those for immunization, the control of (a) diarrheal
diseases, (b) acute respiratory infections, (c) malaria, (d) tuberculosis, and
a variety of specialized activities including those against leprosy, AIDS, and
drug abuse. For most of these activities there will be significant federal as
well as provincial inputs.  Several of these activities receive substantial
external support, especially from UNICEF, USAID and CIDA (USAID' s withdrawal will
partly be compensated by new inputs from ADB).  The governments' plan is to
improve the coverage and effectiveness of these activities. The project will
finance part of this sub-program, mainly through supporting the integration of
most of the various non-specialized vertically organized communicable disease
control programs in the periphery. This will require a major program of re-
training for selected field staff of the key vertical communicable disease
control programs.   Training costs in each province will be provided.  The
peripheral reorganization will also involve parallel changes at the district and
provincial levels including increased delegation of responsibility to the
district level and below. In addition, a system of disease surveillance will be
introduced to facilitate monitoring and upgrading of control strategies. The
majority of, staff and equipment are available and only modest increases are
required. Unlike Balochistan, which generally has sufficient equipment stocks,
much of the equipment in the Puniab will require replacement during the project
period; vehicles in both provinces will be replaced.  Otherwise the types of
input are similar in the two Rrovinc   and the project will finance incremental
staff, transport (vehicles, motorcycles and bicycles), cold chain equipment and
spares, and consumable supplies especially syringes and anti-tuberculosis
medicines. Assurances vere obtained during Negotiations that an in-depth study
for analyzing and saking recommendations on the administrative end staffing
implications of the proposod reorganization of the com=mnicable disease control
program will be completed by July 1994, necessary measures agreed with IDA by
December 1994, and implemontation started by July 1995.
76.   Linked  to  the  above  changes  in communicable  disease  control,  the
sub-program with project financing will strengthen the work of the existing very
modest laboratories in the BHUs/RHCs in both Balochistan and the Puniab through
re-training of existing personnel and provision of additional essential supplies
and equipment including microscopes. Also, unlike in the Punjab, Balochistan has
no effective functioning public health laboratory.   Such a laboratory is
essential for undertaking basic food, water and drug testing for the province.
The project will finance the construction and equipping of a laboratory in
Quetta, staff training and incremental staff and other operating costs.
77.   Making family Rlannlng services available in all health facilities. Almost
all of the governments' population activities are handled by the Ministry of
Population Welfare (MN), with the development plans being presented largely
independently of those for the health system. However, a key part of the Eighth
Plan is that the governments intend to rectify the position whereby most health
facilities do not presently provide family planning services. The project will
finance activities in this crucial but small part of the overall population
program. This sub-program will complement training for health staff already in



- 26 -
progress by MPW and support the introduction of family planning services into all
government health facilities  in both provinces.   Family planning will be
integrated with all other routine health services offered.  Assurances were
obtained during Negotiations that, in advance of the phasing of overall project
implementation by districts, about half of the government health facilities in
Balochistan and the Punjab will offer family planning services by December 1994
and all such facilities will do so by July 1996.
78.   Increasing management and suDervision capacity.  See para. 94.
Imnroved utilization
79.   Through:  (a) strengthening health education; (b) encouraging community
involvement with local health services; and (c) assessing and improving service
impact.
80.   Strengtheninn health education. Health and nutrition education activities
are rudimentary and their development will be a major factor in improving health
status and service utilization. The program anticipates substantial developments
in health education, both federally and provincially, with a particular focus on
MCH. The proposed ADB project will focus especially on the federal level while
FHII will concentrate on the provinces particularly at the district level;
between them the two operations will cover a large proportion of the health
education sub-program. Through FHII, a nucleus of health education professionals
will be established such that there will be a basic public health education
capability in each district by the end of the program.  Education materials
production will normally be contracted out. Health, population and nutrition
education will be integrated into the routine work of all primary health care
staff. MCH and family planning related materials will be shared between the
Health and Population Welfare Departments. Inputs and activities through the
project will be similar in Balochistan and the Punjab and will provide support
for additional professional staff, consultancies, basic equipment and supplies,
and funds for materials research and production.
81.   Encouraginm community involvement with local health services.  Community
involvement in public health services has been seriously neglected. To address
this deficiency, the program intends to make a beginning in both provinces with
project support through:   (a) an information campaign focussed on maternal
health, family planning, communicable diseases and staff recruitment; (b)
appointment of CHWs in the Punjab in consultation with the community; (c)
increasing local 'ownership'  through decentralization (see para. 100);  (d)
experiments with the involvement of Union Councils in the management, review and
funding of BHUs especially in the Punjab; and (e) community financing initiatives
especially for medicines.
82. Assessing and improving service imoact. Through the enhanced management
information system (see para. 101), the impact of increased services utilization
on health status will be assessed, in both provinces, with project financing
through: (a) studies to examine, inter alia, the effects on service utilization



- 27 -
of enhanced service inputs and the impact of changes in public perceptions of
services; and (b) establishing community-based surveillance systems.
Other Primary Health Activities
83.   Other activities included in the provincial programs and therefore in the
annual sectoral reviews, but which will be lower priority sub-programs for FHII
and therefore not financed through the project include: (a) makin; school health
services oRerational - despite the posting of large numbers of doctors to
schools, little progress has been made in either preventive health education or
regular check-ups. During the program period, it is intended to re-orientate the
program to concentrate on promoting healthy life styles combined with a closer
involvement of school staff; (b) strengthening traditional medicine - the
involvement of the public sector in traditional medicine has been limited and
confined to support for training together with the provision of a small number
of curative facilities especially in the Punjab. The program essentially will
continue this approach, but strengthen public sector technical support; and (c)
enhancing non-communicable disease control - the programs focus on two areas,
cancer control and mental health.  For cancer, the intent is to: (i) improve
early detection facilities, especially at district hospitals and above; and (ii)
to establish cancer registries; in mental health, the approach will be to: (i)
decentralize services and integrate them better into primary care; (ii) train the
staff concerned; and (iii) promote mental health education.
Urban Health
84.   The program highlights urban primary health as an area to receive serious
attention especially through establishing and strengthening local government
services.  Substantial funds have been earmarked for this in the development
program, particularly for the creation of a primary health infrastructure.
However, experience with public urban primary health is limited and so the
project will assist with experiments aiming at (a) developing and expanding
prototypes for urban health services delivery and (b) improving cost
effectiveness.   Project investments will be confined to three cities in the
Punjab and limited inputs to one in Balochistan and therefore will account for
only a small part of the total urban primary health sub-program. In the Punjab,
Lahore has successfully developed its primary services, with very limited
resources. It has established a network of primary care facilities, most with
an MCH orientation, and is developing active prevention and promotion programs.
Attempts to improve cost recovery and establish community financing have had some
success. The sub-program will build on this experience through: (a) expanding
these initiatives with a focus on improved service quality and access; (b)
evaluating successful aspects of the system for potential replication elsewhere
in the Punjab; (c) establish a health surveillance system in the Lahore slums;
and (d) experiment further with community financing initiatives and increased
community  involvement.    Project  inputs  include  incremental medicines  and
supplies, construction of houses for female paramedical staff and labor rooms
(which will raise about half of the facilities to the present standard of the
remainder), upgrading and equipping eight diagnostic dispensaries (about half of
the total), equipment, supplies and equipment for the health education and
malaria control programs, and study costs including incremental staff and



- 28 -
consultancies. Additionally, given the large urban population in the Punjab, a
study to develop a scientific typology for urban primary health care in different
sizes of Punjab city will be conducted.   The findings will be tested in
Bahawalpur and Sarghoda towards the end of the program. In the interim, moderate
amounts of micronutrients, syringes and other consumable supplies will be
provided. In Balochistan, the urban population is only substantial in Quetta.
Present primary care services are limited and comprehensive urban health
development plans are yet to be developed. Project inputs are modest and will
support the construction, furnishing and equipping of and supplies for four MCH
Centres (additional to the present six), ambulances and sanitation and malaria
control equipment.
Secondary and Tertiary Services
85.   The governments' programs for the development of hospital services are
still under preparation.  Within the principle of restricting expenditure on
public hospitals, the development program is expected to focus on: (a) limiting
additional beds; (b) controlling the use of expensive new technology; (c)
providing administrative autonomy to larger hospitals; (d) increasing user
charges, with hospitals permitted to retain the collected revenue for subsequent
expenditure on approved items; (e) introducing training for hospital managers;
(f) establishing hospital quality of care control systems; and (g) strengthening
the referral system. Additionally, the government wishes the private sector
gradually to take over the provision of tertiary care. There is no information
on the detailed plans for either Balochistan or the Punjab. The project is not
proposing to finance inputs to hospitals per se, other than those for ensuring
a minimum of obstetric emergency and first level referral cover together with
some support service inputs. Selected bilateral donors are expected to finance
limited inputs to hospitals especially for medical equipment, while the ADB is
planning to upgrade critical secondary curative services particularly in the
Punjab. In addition, there are other continuing donor supported programs, in
such areas as laboratory development and medical equipment maintenance and
repair, which are mainly hospital oriented.
Staff Develoment'M (total project contribution US$31.2 million, of which
Balochistan  US$9.9  million  and  the  Punjab  US$21.3  million,  including
contingencies)
86.   The provincial programs recognize that improvement in the quality and
availability of health staff, particularly female paramedical staff, is the key
to making progress in the sector. The strategy in both provinces is similar and
aims at improving staff capabilities and performance and increasing the number
of female paramedical staff through: (a) introducing systematic in-service
training to refresh and improve the skills of existing primary health care staff;
(b) increasing the training capacity for female paramedical staff and
strengthening and expanding existing training institutions and programs; and (c)
improving the recruitment and retention of selected groups of key female
paramedical staff. A key constraint to improving the availability and use of
W  Further details an this coponent are given in Aznex 5.



- 29 -
maternal health services is the limited number of female paramedical staff,
especially in Balochistan. The programs include the provision of comprehensive
training   to   improve   existing   staff  capabilities,   performance,   and
motivation271. Past in-service training in both provinces has been limited and
ad hoc. The proposed continuous in-service training program has been planned so
that training for different categories of staff is complementary and that in-
service training and supervision are linked. In order for these approaches to
be effective, female staff must begin service after training and be retained.
The ADB Health Care Development project and FHII are especially complementary in
the staff development area, and between them will cover almost all of the
programs' requirements for paramedical staff development in both Rrovinces.
While medical staff will be beneficiaries of the in-service training, the
presently available provincial programs do not address directly either: (a) the
problem of the over-supply and poor quality of new medical graduates; or (b) the
in-service training needs of hospital-based medical staff. While accepting that
(a) is an important issue, neither the federal nor provincial governments have
yet been able to develop plans which will command the necessary political support
to tackle the matter directly; (b) is a complex matter which has yet to be
seriously taken up by any government.
Inproving staff caRabilities and Rerformance
87.   The programs intend to introduce in a phased manner a comprehensive,
sustained and well-organized job-specific in-service training program for
existing primary health care staff.  Key staff will receive some in-service
training at least once a year. In-service training programs will be developed
at the provincial level through curriculum development workshops conducted with
technical assistance especially during the first year. District level trainers
will be trained by provincial master trainers.   The in-service training
activities will be coordinated and partly implemented through the Provincial and
District Health Development Centres (see para. 94). Training for RHC and BHU
staff in the training and supervision of lower level workers is also incorporated
as are the re-training programs to better integrate the staff of the communicable
disease control programs (see para. 75). In each province, about 30 different
in-service training programs are envisaged ranging from one day seminars to one
month courses with participants varying from physicians to TBAs and CHWs. While
some in-service training will continue under the auspices of specific programs,
especially the centrally funded vertical programs, the project will finance a
very substantial part of the overall in-service training sub-program.   The
implementation of this in-service training sub-program is a large undertaking and
it is essential that key trainers are in position early in the program period.
Assurances were obtained during Negotiations that the provincial staff
development teams, which will be responsible for developing the provincial in-
service training programs, will be established and adequately staffed by December
1993.

will involve all tyma of primary hoalth car. staff, male and femalc.

Increasing the output and auality of selected groups of paramedical

88.   The program intends to address the present shortage of key paramedical
staff through a major expansion to the existing provincial training capacity by:
(a) establishing a substantial number of small new training schools; and (b)
strengthening and expanding existing training institutions and programs. This
will include activities to improve the quality of pre-service training. Detailed
provincial plans for these developments have been prepared which will meet the
provinces' needs for additional paramedical staff for the next ten years. ADB
and FHII between them will meet virtually the entire needs of both provinces,
with ADB expected to finance a substantial majority.
89.   Establishing additional paramedical schools.  The requirements in the two
provinces differ. In the Punjab, the projected staffing needs of the province
require that 24 new institutions will be established. All of these new schools
will be financed by the ADB except for two lady health visitor schools, at
Chakwal and Sahiwal, which will be financed through FHII. The two FHII financed
schools will eventually produce some 100 new lady health visitors annually. The
project will provide for the construction, equipment, furniture, transport and
contribute to the schools' incremental operating costs.  In Balochistan, the
needs are more extreme, the constraints much greater and the numbers required
less. The provincial plan calls for the establishment of seven new schools, all
of which will be ADB financed.
90.   Strengthening existing traininA  institutions and 2rograms.   In bo_th
Rrovinces, training quality improvement activities will include curriculum
revision, training trainers, teaching materials and equipment provision, and
improved testing procedures. To help address the chronic shortages of female
staff in Balochistan, in addition to expanding the TBA training program, a new
program will be introduced to enhance the skill of the dais (low level female
staff already working in many rural health facilities).   In addition,  in
Balochistan five and in the Puniab 15, existing training facilities require
modest expansion and additional female supervisory staff in order to meet
additional training requirements especially in midwifery. FHII will finance the
Balochistan and three of the Punjab school expansions while ADB will finance the
remainder. In addition, the Punjab program includes provision for upgrading the
College of Community Medicine in Lahore; the College is the only post-basic
training institution in public health in Pakistan. FHII will meet a small part
of this, mainly through technical support. Overall, FHII will finance building
upgrading, two hostels, staff housing, equipment, transport for students and
supervision, training supplies, technical assistance and help with incremental
staff and operating costs.

Izproving trainee recruitment schemes, incentives and iob enhancement
lDro&r msL
91.   Female staff recruitment and retention problems are acute in Balochistan
and therefore the government has included in its plans an intensive effort to
address  these  difficulties.    Steps  include  establishing  motivation  and
recruitment teams and campaigns, using mass media, faculty-family visits and
parent education programs; enhancing the training stipends, salary steps, bonus
systems, and service conditions and incentives, particularly for rural service;
and housing and transport availability and security for female paramedical staff
both during training and service. In addition, work will continue on improving
the definition of career paths for female staff, integration of cadres with
similar training, and making minimum rural service and rotation requirements
clear. Similar measures, but at a greatly reduced level, are planned for the
Punjab where these problems are much less severe.  Assurances were obtained
during Negotiations that a study of the financial and non-financial incentives
and service conditions of female paramedical staff, both during training and
subsequent employment, vill be conducted for each province by July 1994,
necessary measures agreed vith IDA and implementation started by December 1994.
These reviews will take account of the similar work in progress under the Family
Health Project in NVYP and Sindh.
Management and Organizational Development~w (total project contribution
US$13.1 million, of which Balochistan US$4.0 million and the Punjab US$9.1
million, including contingencies).
92.   The objective of this important sub-program is to improve health management
capabilities through (a) increased management and planning institutional
capacity; and (b) improved management quality and efficiency.   Strengthened
management will facilitate the effective utilization of investments throughout
the sector, but particularly at the district level and below.  Broadly, the
project will finance almost all of the programs' provincial management
development activities.
Increased manatement and planning institutional capacity
93.   The provincial programs will increase management capacity through:  (a)
establishing provincial and district management development units; (b)
strengthening provincial planning capacity; and (c) strengthening provincial
implementation capacity.
94.   Establishing provincial and district management develoRment units.  Since
neither in-service training nor management development are presently recognized
as line activities of the provincial health departments, it will be necessary for
these important activities to be institutionalized during the program period.
To this end, a Provincial Health Development Centre (PHDC) will be established
in both  Balochistan  and  the  Puniab  comprising  four  units:    management
development,   staff  development,   health  and  nutrition  education   and
I Further details are given in Annex 5.



- 32 -
research/monitoring. The basic operational strategy for all these functions is
in-service training reinforced by supportive supervision.  The PHDC for the
Punjab will be in Lahore and be a development and expansion of the existing
Management Training Institute for Doctors; in Balochistan, the PHDC will be
established in Quetta. In addition, a modest District Health Development Centre
(DHDC) will be established initially in each division headquarters and
subsequently in each district headquarters in the Punjab and in each division
headquarters in Balochistan to assist the district team's work and to provide
some  limited  residential  in-service  training  facilities.30/   The  PHDC,
supported by the DHDCs, will be responsible for facilitating the implementation
of much of the province's training program, both for technical in-service and
management training. Further details of the structure and functions of the PHDC
and DHDCs are given in Annex 6.
95.   Within this framework, detailed requirements and hence the inputs vary
significantly between Balochistan and the Punjab. The project will finance all
of these activities including civil works for constructing the two PHDCs and for
establishing about 36 DHDCs (6 in Balochistan and 30 in the Punjab) plus their
office equipment and furniture, training equipment and supplies. Mobility is
essential for the district teams and about 46 vehicles, mostly 4-wheel drive, for
both provinces will be provided. Funds are also included for teaching materials,
course costs, local and foreign consultants, fellowships, incremental salaries
and allowances, and vehicle operating costs. Officers have been identified for
the three senior positions in each Provincial Health Development Centre.
Assurances were obtained during Negotiations that: (a) at least 75 percent of
the professional staff of the Provincial Health Development Centres will be
recruited or transferred in by the end of December 1993 with the Centres being
fully staffed by December 1994; and (b) that at least 75 percent of the
professional staff for the District Health Development Centres will be recruited
or transferred in by the end of the first year in which they are scheduled to
begin operations with the balance appointed during the second year of operation.
96.   Strengtheninag  rovincial planning capacity.  Only very limited health
planning is presently done, but both provinces have embryonic planning cells
within the Health Departments. Building on this small base, the program will
strengthen their capacity considerably and eventually establish a viable health
planning capability in each province. In Balochistan, where a start has already
been made with ADB and ODA support, the sub-program will supplement this through
FHII. In the Punlab, FHII will finance the entire activity. The development of
the planning capacity will be closely related to the parallel improvements in
management and organization and with the establishment of manpower planning
capacity (ADB assisted). FHII will provide for vehicles, office equipment and
furniture, fellowships, consultants, and assistance with incremental staff and
operating costs.
9/ Restricting DBDC to the division level in alj ofhLJtn msinly reflects the relatively small number
of health staff in each district compared to the YZ1^> Subsequent references to district Centres end
tems should be read as applying to the division level in flcckaLVM.



- 33 -
97.   Strengthenia Rrov  cal LUnementation capcitv. To support the existing
line management who will be responsible for implementing the program and sub-
programs, in both provinces, the project will finance essential additional staff,
supplies, transport and office equipment. Provision for training for staff and
for regular bi-annual inter-provincial coordination meetings is included. To
assist the development of any follow-up operation, an allocation for the
preparation of such projects is included.
Imnroved manatement guality and efficiency
98.   Through:  (a) introducing systematic management training; (b) increasing
decentralization of authority to the district level; (c) developing monitoring,
information and financial systems; and (d) strengthening supervisory skills and
practices.
99.   Introducing systematic management trainin.g  An in-service management and
supervisory training system will be developed so that, from the district level
down, primary care service managers in both Rrovinces receive an initial
intensive program with regular refresher courses. About 75X of managers will
receive initial training by the end of the third year. This program is closely
linked to the above  technical  in-service  training programs.   To ensure
consistency of district staff development, regular in-service training and
planning workshops will be held for the district teams themselves, mainly
conducted by provincial level staff.
100.  Increasina decentralization of authority to the district level.    The
delegation of increased program and financial authority to the district level
proposed in the Seventh Plan has yet to be implemented.  The programs make
allowance for this and the Departments of Health are currently studying the
problem with the intent to increase delegation levels, linked to enhanced
accountability, insofar as it is within their administrative power. The project
will provide technical assistance to facilitate this process. Assurances were
obtained during Negotiations that:  (a) powers will be delegated to District
Health Officers by June 1994 to hire, transfer and promote staff up to Basic Pay
Scale 12 in their districts; and (b) existing levels of financial delegation for
operating expenditures will be implemented.
101.  Developing monitorin. information and financial sstems. The sub-program
plans that these systems will be linked to the operational and budget systems in
order to record service statistics and expenditure against plans; having both the
planned and actual information will best support management decision making.
Existing information systems are weak but with USAID assistance, a significant
start on strengthening them has been made. In both provinces the programs will
build on this and focus on establishing a functioning district level information
system. The financial monitoring and budget preparation process of the health
sector will also be strengthened through analyzing the present systems and
procedures used, implementing improvements, and enhancing routine monitoring.
The main project financed inputs will be for staff training, computers and
software, publications and technical assistance.



- 34 -
102. Strenntheninn suRervisorv skills and gractices. The approach in the sub-
program is that, after initial training at the PHDC and after improved
supervisory procedures are developed, a member from each district team will visit
all health facilities in their respective portion of the district, typically
monthly in the Punjab but less frequently in Balochistan, for a combination of
in-service training and supervision. The district team will also be responsible
for planning and supervising the development of the health system in their
district through annual plans linked to the budget cycle, for maintenance,
logistics, and equipment provision.
Other Support Areas
103.  The program expects to develop activities in three other areas:   (a)
experiments in health sector financing - in order to mobilize additional
resources for the sector, it is planned to:  (i) expand the coverage of the
existing social security system; (ii) introduce and increase user charges in
tertiary hospitals; and (iii) introduce a health insurance system in the larger
cities; (b) increase domestic groduction of oharmaceuticals and medical equipment
- this matter is currently under review by a federal Cabinet committee and plans
will be developed after the committee makes its recommendations; and (c)
strategies to encourage the further develoRment of the Rrivate health sector -
this mainly concerns hospitals, with plans for increasing involvement in primary
care yet to be developed.   The project will not directly finance these sub-
programs since they are either already financed or a low priority for primary
health service development.
Federal  Level   (total  project  contribution  US$5.2  million,   including
contingencies)
104. Federal activities included in FHII are discrete selected investments and
focus on: (a) improving primary health care in the Islamabad Capital Territory
(ICT); and (b) establishing health systems research capacity.
Imoroved Rrimarv health care ln the federal capital area
105. Through: (a) the development of primary care activities in the ICT; and
(b) strengthening one NGO female paramedical training school.
106. The develoment of Rrimarv care activities in ICT. In the rural parts of
the ICT, the health infrastructure is generally in place but the health system
needs considerable strengthening before it can provide quality primary
(especially HCH) services linked to existing referral facilities. Based on the
experience of a pilot scheme, the project will undertake a range of activities
similar to, but more intensive than, those in the two provinces including
introduction of a CHW scheme, training, reorganization and strengthening of CDC
activities, management development, and monitoring and evaluation.   Greater
intensity of supervision and evaluation will facilitate experimentation and
detailed epidemiological studies. Major inputs include equipment, transport and
communications, incremental staff and operating costs, and technical assistance.



- 35 -
107.  Strenathenina one NGO female paramedical school.  This lady health visitor
school is the only one run by an NGO. It is well-established but operates from
inadequate rented buildings with very limited hostel facilities. The project
will finance the construction of a school, MCH clinic and hostel for 60 students,
furniture, teaching equipment and student transport.
108.  Health systems research cagacitX strenathenina.  Within the country there
is very limited capacity in health systems research. One result is that there
is no clear basis on which to make systems related planning, management and
investment decisions.  In order to build such research capacity, the project
will, in association with the Pakistan Medical Research Council, establish
approval mechanisms, provide research funds, assist with proposal development and
results dissemination, and provide training and technical assistance.
109. As an integral part of all three components of the project, provision is
made for a modest program of domestic and overseas fellowshiRp. These will be
awarded, for agreed subjects and durations, following standard government
procedures.  Consultant services, to supplement and fill gaps in federal and
provincial knowledge and experience, are provided. While considerable expertise
exists within the country and will be tapped by the project (about 370 m/m
equivalent), international consultants in selected areas will also be required
(about 330 m/m). Provision for studies and applied research, both at the federal
and provincial level, is also included.
D. Environmental Considerations
110. The proposed project was determined to be in screening Category C which
includes projects that are expected to have no significant negative environmental
impact. No environmental analysis has been prepared for this project.

112.  In the Punjab, total health development expenditures have remained almost
constant in real terms in the past four years but are expected to increase during
the Plan period due  to allocations  for  the  Social Action  Program.   A
disaggregated analysis of the different categories of expenditure reveals a
change towards greater emphasis on preventive care (an increase from 0.6X of
total health development expenditures to 17.2X during the Plan)33/, when
comparing the average allocations during 1989/90-1992/93 with the Plan figures.
This is a welcome development since a greater focus on preventive care would be
desirable on both efficiency and equity grounds. A similar comparison for rural
health shows a decline from 37X to 281. However, a year-by-year analysis reveals
that the 28X figure is a reversal of a trend over the past four years where rural
health expenditures had reached a low of 17.51 in 1992/93 due to the completion
of the rural health infrastructure construction program.   Expenditures on
hospitals and urban primary services combined would increase slightly from 25X
to 28X of the total, while there would be a noticeable decline in the share of
human resource development from 251 to 171.  The latter reflects the near
completion of medical school infrastructure development that dominated the past
two decades. Thus, in general, the preliminary broad functional distribution of
health development expenditures under the Eighth Plan in the Punjab represents
a move in the right direction. Realization of this move will be monitored during
the scheduled annual reviews.
113. In BalcKhistan, total health development expenditures have fluctuated
during the past four years in real terms, from a decline of 381 between 1989/90-
1990/91 to almost double between 1991/92-1992/93. While this volatility reflects
the much smaller total figures involved compared to the Punjab, there is
nevertheless, an upward trend, which is expected to continue in the Plan period.
Looking at the categories of expenditure, the average development expenditure for
the past four years for preventive care and rural health were 181 and 471
respectively, but they are expected to decline during the Plan period (141 and
291 respectively). The sharp decline in the share of rural health care is due
to the tapering off of the rural health facility construction program. The share
of hospitals and urban primary services combined is expected to remain steady at
around 221 of development expenditures, having declined steadily from a high of

114. Table 3.1 also shows how the expenditures under FHII will fit in the entire
health development programs of the two provinces. In the Pmujab, expenditures
under the proposed project would represent about 14.61 of the total program, but
their importance would be greater in the high-priority categories: 18X in
preventive care, 201 in rural health, 22.31 in human resource development and
only 51 in hospitals. In Balochistan, expenditures under the proposed project
would represent a much higher overall share of the total program than in the
Punjab, at about 401.  They would be a proportionally greater part of rural
health (521) and human resource development (721), and these two categories would
comprise about 761 of FHII expenditures in the province (Rs. 426 million out of
a total of Rs. 562 million).
Currnt KzDenditures
115. Unlike the case with development expenditures, which are projected by the
Government in a five year cycle in the context of the Plans, there are no
official projections of current expenditures other than in the annual budgets.
Table 3.2 presents a set of projections of current health expenditures for the
Punjab and Balochistan to 1998/99, taking 1992/93 as the benchmark year. These
projections are based on agreements that the Bank would expect to reach with the
Government of Pakistan on provincial current health expenditures, in the context
of the proposed Public Sector Adjustment Loan (PSAL).34/ The agreements would
be: (a) to at least maintain current expenditures on health services in real per
capita terms (implying an annual real rate of increase of 31 at the
minimum)35/; (b) to raise current expenditures on kbsic health services by at
least 21 in real per capita terms annually (implying an annual real rate of
increase of at least 5X); and (c) to maintain the share of the non-salary
component of current expenditure on basic health services (221 and 27X for the
Punjab and Balochistan, respectively).
116. Total current health expenditure for both the Punjab and Balochistan has
been growing in the past three years in real terms.  In the former province,
growth was 41, 91 and 221 for successive years after 1989/90 and in the latter
province, expenditures grew at 3.51, 141 and 16X after 1989/90.
117. As shown in Table 3.2, the agreements proposed to be reached in the context
of the PSAL imply that the share of basic health services as a proportion of
total current health expenditures in the Punjab would increase from 311 in
1992/93 to 351 in 1998/99; and in Balohisa, the corresponding increase would
be from 421 to 471. However, the projections in Table 3.2 represent the minimum

desirable levels of current health expenditures in the two provinces; if GDP
continues to grow at about 5-6X per year in real terms, as it has in the past
decade, it is expected that total current health expenditures would grow at a
rate that at least matches the growth in GDP, with current expenditure on basic
health services growing at an even higher rate.
118. A very rough estimate of the recurrent cost implications of health
development expenditures envisaged during the Eighth Plan period for the Punjab
suggests that the growth in current expenditures projected in Table 3.2 would be
insufficient.  The incremental recurrent costs of FHII imply that by 1998/99 (the
first year after project completion) about Rs. 152.5 million (in 1992/93 prices)
would have to be added to the current health budget of this province in order to
adequately sustain the activities started under the project. Since the project
represents only about 14.6X of the total Eighth Plan health development program
of the Punjab, a very rough estimate of the recurrent cost implications of the
entire program equals Rs. 1,045 million (Rs. 153 million/0.146). Compared to the
projected increase of total current health expenditure of Rs. 691 million shown
in Table 3.2 (Rs. 4,251 million - Rs. 3,560 million), a 'deficit of Rs. 354
million would be expected by 1989/99. This figure is probably on the high side,
since it is expected that the project would fund a higher proportion of software
activities than the total program, which would upwardly bias the above estimate
of current expenditures. An alternative method of calculating the 'deficit',
based on the assumption that the rest of the investment program at the end of the
Plan period is expected to generate about 70X35/ of that generated by the
project, produces a 'deficit' more in the order of Rs. 86 million at the end of
the project period. Thus, an annual real rate of growth higher than 31 would be
required. Project assurances have been developed which allow for this.
119. The growth of the development program under the Eighth Plan would not be
the only source of (desirable) growth of health current expenditure in the
period: even without any additions to the present health system in terms of
coverage and range of services, current expenditures on health would need to
increase over time on account of heavier use of existing facilities due to the
current budget backlog" affecting many of these facilities at present levels of
utilization.   Thus an annual real rate of growth of total current health
expenditure of 3X seems insufficient for Punjab. What would be an adequate rate
of growth? This question cannot be answered with the available information; for
illustrative purposes, however, if total current health expenditure in the Punjab
grew at an annual rate of 61, the total increase over the period up to 1998/99
would be equal to Rs. 1,490 million, which is likely to be closer to an adequate
level than that resulting from 31 annual growth.
120.  In Balochistan the situation is not as stark.   The annual incremental
recurrent costs resulting from FHII in 1998/99 would amount to Rs. 57 million (in
1992/93 prices); suggesting that the recurrent cost implications of the entire
development program by that year are of the order of Rs. 144 million, which is
close to the total current health expenditures projected in Table 3.2 of Rs. 136
million.   Using the alternative method mentioned above, the 701 assumption
I The 701 assumptioa is estimtod from the investmnt prosrm of the Kiahth Plan.



- 40 -
produces a 'surplus' of Rs. 18 million. Hence, in contrast to the Punjab, the
funding requirements for the current budget 'backlog in Balochistan are less
significant.
121. In addition to the government budgets, Zakat funds provide a safety net for
providing medicines and consumable supplies to the poor. In 1989/90 Zakat funds
amounted to Rs. 37 million and Rs. 3 million for the Punjab and Balochistan
respectively, and they have doubled in 1991/92. Also, policy measures likely to
be pursued through the SAP for tertiary care facilities, including raising
additional resources through levying user charges and making selected facilities
more autonomous, would release resources which could be allocated to primary
care.
122. The incremental recurrent costs of the program have been minimized through
careful program design and through the focus on better utilization of past
investments, especially  in  infrastructure.   However, the  estimated  total
incremental costs are such that increases in the total budget, combined with some
modest reallocations during the program period toward rural health and preventive
programs and away from hospital-related expenditures, would be required. This
would be monitored as part of the annual review of the program.

million.  Price contingencies37/ (US$11.9 million) reflect Bank guidelines on
expected local and international price increases. The foreign exchange component
is estimated at US$49.7 million or 44X of total project costs. Costs include
incremental recurrent expenditure.   A summary of costs by components and

124. Basis of Cost Estimates. The costs of civil works are based on reviews of
recently awarded contracts in Balochistan and the Punjab for similar construction
and are about Rs. 370 per square foot. Estimates are based on final designs for
standardized works and on preliminary designs for other types of construction.
Costs of equipment, furniture, transport and supplies are based on analyses of
requirements for the project and derive from local market prices or CIF unit
prices, both adjusted to include local transportation and installation,
supplemented by recent experience of purchasing similar goods in Balochistan and
the Punjab. Costs of production of education materials, training, local and
expatriate consultants, applied research and other services reflect local or
international prices as applicable. Overseas fellowships are based on averaged
United Nations rates for training in Europe and North America. Costs of salaries
and allowances, including training allowances, are based on current Government
rates. Costs are based on June 1992 prices and include adjustments for price and
foreign exchange rate changes between project appraisal and negotiations.
125.  Customs Dutes and Taxes.  Costs include an estimated US$14.5 million in
direct and indirect taxes and duties on civil works, goods and services, to be
financed by the Government.
126.  Continaency allowances.  Physical contingencies are estimated at lOX for
civil works, equipment, furniture, training, operating costs, education
materials, contraceptives and medicines and supplies, and 51 for vehicles,
technical assistance and studies.   Overall, they total US$7.3 million for
investment and recurrent costs and represent 7.71 of baseline costs or 6.4X of
total costs. Price contingencies by the end of implementation are estimated at
US$11.9 million on base cost plus physical contingencies. Total contingencies
represent 20.31 of the base cost.

127.  Foreign exchznge conMonent.  The foreign exchange component is US$49.7
million including contingencies, or 44X of total project costs.  It includes
indirect foreign cost, and is estimated as follows: 9X for civil works; 65X for
equipment; 501 for vehicles; 151 for furniture; 751 for foreign consultant
services; 1001 for overseas fellowships; 100X for contraceptives; 51 for local
technical assistance; 501 for medicines and 40X for other supplies; 201 for
education materials; and 50X for operating costs (including vehicles).  These
percentages are based on the expectation that: (i) all civil works contracts
would be awarded to local firms and most building materials would be produced
locally; (ii) 751 of medical equipment and 501 of other equipment and vehicles
would be imported; and (iii) 851 of furniture would be purchased locally.
128. Incremental recurrent costs generated by project implementation during the
five-year period (including contingencies) are estimated at US$33.0 million, of
which US$13.0 million (39X) is for salaries and allowances, US$13.5 million (411)
is for medicines and supplies, and US$6.5 million (201) is for other incremental
recurrent expenses such as consumable education materials and vehicle operating
costs.   The estimated annual incremental recurrent cost for financial year
1998/99 is US$2. 3 million and US$6.1 million respectively for Balochistan and the
Punjab, or about 8.81 and 4.31 of their 1992/93 recurrent budgets. Recurrent
costs and budgets were discussed further above (see para. 115).
129. Flow of funds. The donor funds for the provincial sub-programs will be
channelled through the standard government system for large externally financed
development projects, with the balance for the provincial governments'
contributions coming from the provincial development programs. The provinces
will make funds available as a grant to the four urban local bodies and to the
NGOs involved. The small federal component will be wholly budgeted through the
federal development program with funds passed on as grants to the NGOs, local
bodies and research organizations involved.
130. Prolect financing. The total project cost of US$114.0 million equivalent
will be financed by the Government, IDA, the British Overseas Development
Administration (ODA), and the German Kreditanstalt fur Wiederaufbau (KfW). The
proposed IDA credit of US$48.0 million equivalent will finance about 421 of total
project cost including 501 of the local costs (US$32.2 million). Under parallel
arrangements, (a) ODA will finance US$12.2 million equivalent mainly for
technical assistance in Balochistan and the Punjab, and part of the Lahore urban
health services subcomponent; and (b) Germany will finance US$22.2 million
equivalent for investment costs for equipment in both provinces plus the NGO
subcomponents. Together, the IDA credit and the parallel financing will cover
1001 of the foreign exchange component (US$49.7 million) and 511 of the local
costs (US$32.8 million).  The Government will finance the balance of project
costs, including taxes and duties (US$31.6 million). The costs include up to
US$0.5 million for retroactively financed activities from July 1992 (appraisal)
to meet the cost of equipment, vehicles, training, project coordination and
technical assistance needed for a timely project start. This will enable the
executing agencies to finalize technical assistance agreements, prepare

engineering surveys for the first year construction program and train key staff.

C. Prolect Nanaaement and Imnlementation3'/
131. With the exception of federal level activities, the program (including the
priority sub-prograws financed by the project) will be implemented by line
agencies of the Balochistan and the Punjab provincial governments, reinforced
with  technical assistance and selected institutional modifications.    The
principal implementing agencies will be the provincial Departments of Health.
At the most senior level, to provide a more structured forum than presently
exists for interdepartmental and inter-organization coordination and to guide
program implementation, provincial program Steering Co.sittees have been
established in Balochistan and the Punjab. Working level provincial technical
com ittees will also be established that will meet frequently and address
operational matters.
132. In each province, the provincial Department of Health will be responsible
for implementing the program. The line managers in the DOH will be responsible
for all program implementation within their respective areas of responsibility,
including any project supported sub-programs. To strengthen the existing line
management, additional resources will be made available especially to assist
with:  (a) ensuring that budgets, accounts, audits, bidding, procurement and
disbursement operations are conducted in accordance with donor requirements;
(b) monitoring compliance with the Credit Agreement and other donor agreements;
/ Further details are given in Annex 6.



- 45 -
(c) monitoring program progress including the construction activities implemented
by the Department of Communications and Works; and (d) ensuring that new
activities for the DOH (such as management development) begin and that the
associated institutional structure is in place.    The Punjab government has
established a special directorate for coordinating the execution of external
assistance391 under the guidance of an Executive Director.   Balochistan is
making similar arrangements.    The staff directly concerned with project
activities include a Director, Deputy Director, and support personnel. Project
Directors and some staff have already been appointed and transferred-in; the
remainder will be appointed as project work increases during 1993.   In
Balochistan, the appointment of the Project Director and Deputy Project Director
and any subsequent replacements will be subject to the no objection of the
Additional Chief Secretary. A diagram summarizing the provincial implementation
arrangements is at Chart 2.
133. Under the overall guidance of the provincial Director General of Health
Services, the Deputy Director for Basic Health Services (DDBHS) has overall
responsibility for Rrimary health services enacting the programs in close
collaboration with the other Deputy Directors concerned, and with the Inspectress
of Health Services4*0  (maternal and child health and family planning) (in the
Puniab, her senior staff have recently been strengthened). In-service training
and management development will be mainly implemented through the Provincial
Health Development Centre (PHDC) and the associated District Health Development
Centres (DHDC).411 The urban health subcomponents will be implemented by the
head of the medical department of each of the Metropolitan/Municipal Corporations
(MC). The NGOs concerned will implement their respective subcomponents, with an
existing senior officer having responsibility for coordination. To ensure that
implementation starts on schedule, project launch workshops will be held
facilitated by IDA and the cooperating donors.    Detailed implementation
arrangements for the three provincial components are given in Annex 6.
134. Key technical support for the PHDC, DHDCs, and for project implementation
generally will be provided by local academic institutions combined with
international support.  This will consist of technical advice and long-term
support to crucial areas for project implementation, particularly the (a)
management development and (b) staff development activities of the PHDCs and the
technical underpinning for the health services development component. Subjects
to be addressed include management information systems, training of trainers,
curricula development, project planning, health education, and community
involvement. In the Punjab, the Lahore University of Management Sciences (LUMS)
and the Lahore College of Community Medicine,, supported by an international
institution, will provide technical assistance in training, health services and

with local universities and non-governmental organizations for obtaining
technical support for the project by the provinces vill begin during the first
project year and continue throughout the project; (b) agreements for the

135. The implementation of the small federal level component will be the
responsibility of: (a) for the Islamabad Capital Territory (ICT), where health
services are currently the responsibility of three organizations (the Ministry
of Health (MOH), the Capital Development Authority and the Islamabad
Administration), the subcomponent will be coordinated by an FHII Director and
staff who will report to a coordinating committee composed of representatives of
the three organizations.  By the third project year, it is intended that a
unified system of health services for the ICT will be in-place under the
leadership of the MOH; (b) the health systems research subcomponent will be
coordinated by the same Project Director and staff, with MOH taking the lead but
working closely with the Pakistan Medical Research Council, with research schemes
approved through a system of national and provincial research committees; and (c)
the NGO project in Islamabad will be implemented similarly to the provincial NGO
projects but under the auspices of the MOH.
136. In accordance with standard government practice in Pakistan, the respective
Balochistan and Punjab Departments of Communications and Works (C&W) will be
responsible for the civil works aspects of the programs (including FHII) to be
implemented by the two provincial governments including site surveys, preparation
of line plans, working drawings, and specifications and tender documents. Once
contracts are awarded, C&W will provide site supervision and progress reports.
The supervision costs, including transport and supplementary staff where
necessary, will be borne by the program (including project financed sub-
programs).   Both provincial C&W departments have  the necessary  technical
competence to implement the modest level of civil works financed through the
project. The MCs will follow their standard procedures, which are very similar
to those of the provincial governments. NGOs will implement their respective
subcomponents, assisted by consultants for major construction. In Islamabad, the
Capital Development Authority will undertake the functions analogous to the
provincial C&Ws. Further details on project construction are given in Annex 6.
137. Almost all construction will be at existing DOH, MC or NGO owned locations.
Proof of ownership of sites for all major new construction including the public
health laboratory in Quetta, the two new public health schools in the Punjab and
the two PHDCs has been provided, as well as for the first two years construction
program of DHDCs.   Proof of ownership of sites for the two NGO maternity
hospitals and schools and for the NGO public health school has also been
provided. Any funds required for the acquisition of further DHDC sites will be



- 47 -
provided through the provincial budgets.  Line plans have been prepared and
reviewed by IDA for all major new construction. Assurances vere obtained during
Negotiations that all remaining sites will be acquired and available by June
1995.
138. Nonitorina and evaluation. The provincial DOHs will monitor and report
independently every six months, typically in February and August, to IDA and the
cooperating donors on project progress. The provincial reports will be based on
reports from the districts and from the provincial departments and directorates
concerned, in addition to regular monitoring. Reporting will be structured for
each major project financed intervention with routine monitoring limited to
indicators of inputs, activities and resultant outputs; the main indicators that
will be used are summarized in Annex 3.   Implementation problems will be
highlighted, together with proposed solutions. DOH monitoring and evaluation
capabilities will be substantially strengthened through the development of the
Management Information System and the establishment of the Research, Monitoring
and Evaluation Units in the PHDCs and DHDCs. A number of special evaluation
studies are planned including, for example, a study of the impact of improved
health services in the Punjab and of service quality in Balochistan. Technical
assistance will support program and project monitoring. A joint government, IDA
and cooperating donor mid-term review of the project will be held in early 1996.
The provincial Planning and Development Departments will also review progress.
A Project Completion Report will be prepared. Assurances were obtained during
Negotiations that:  (a) semi-annual progress reports will be prepared by the
Departments of Health and submitted to IDA; (b) a joint government and IDA mid-
term review will be held before May 1996; and (c) a Project Completion Report
will be submitted by the governments to IDA within six months of the closing date
of the project.
139. Development projects in Pakistan must be presented to the approving
government authorities in a prescribed detailed format, known as a PC-1.  A
project such as this does not fit easily into the PC-1 format since the more
programmatic approach and the implementation of the many different small sub-
programs requires more flexibility than is typically permitted in a PC-1. Before
Negotiations, draft PC-ls for the project were prepared, reviewed to ensure that
they were satisfactory to IDA and approved by the provincial and federal421
governments. Approval of the project PC-ls by the Executive Committee of the
Federal Government's National Economic Council would be a condition of Credit
Effectiveness.
140.  IDA SuRervision Requirements.  The project will require intensive IDA
supervision, particularly in the first years of operation, to help ensure that
project start-up and early implementation proceed as scheduled. Initial annual
supervision requirements are estimated to be 18 staff-weeks. A supervision plan
for the project is at Annex 11.

D.  Procurement.
141. Procurement will be conducted separately for each province and the federal
level.  Procurement methods are summarized in Table 3.6 below.  The two C&W
Departments have the administrative capacity and experience to procure a variety
of small and medium sized construction contracts in both urban and remote rural
areas which comprise the project. Experience in both provinces has shown that
larger local contractors have not shown interest in bidding on small scattered
contracts scheduled at different times during project implementation. Therefore,
the civil works program will be procured as small individual contracts (average
contract size is estimated at US$50,000 equivalent) with groupings of contracts,
where geographic proximity and project scheduling permit, particularly for urban
areas, to encourage the participation of larger local contractors.
a. All contracts for clvll works financed by IDA, at an estimated total
cost of US$13.6 million, will be awarded through local competitive
bidding, in accordance with procedures which are acceptable to the
Association.   IDA's prior review will be required for the first
contract in each province and for all individual civil works contracts
exceeding US$200,000 (about eight contracts representing 33X of IDA
financed civil works); other contracts will be subject to post-award
reviews. About US$3.3 million of civil works contracts financed by KfW
and ODA will be awarded following KfW and ODA standard procedures.
b. Equipment is generally for medical purposes, and comprises small
clinical equipment, kits for paramedical staff, syringes and other
medical accessories which will be procured annually based on actual
needs. Specialized medical equipment will be procured internationally.
Medicines  and  consumable   supplies   generally  comprise  common
pharmaceuticals, reagents and micronutrients which will be procured
annually based on actual needs. To the extent practicable, contracts
for equlpment, furniture, medlclnes and contraceptlves financed by IDA
with an estimated aggregate value of US$14.4 million, will be grouped
to permit bulk procurement of at least US$200,000 equivalent.
Contracts estimated to cost more than US$200,000 will be awarded on the
basis of ICB, in accordance with Bank guidelines. Contracts of less
than US$200,000 each, not exceeding US$1.7 million in total, will be
awarded on the basis of LCB procedures acceptable to IDA. Items which
cannot be grouped into packages of at least US$50,000, which in
aggregate will not exceed US$1.2 million, will be procured using local
or international shopping. Medicines and minor medical equipment are
expected to be procured from UNICEF up to a total of US$5.3 million,
and contraceptives from UNFPA up to a total of US$6.7 million. About
US$25.9 million equivalent of contracts for equipment, furniture and
medicines, financed by KfW and ODA, wil.l be awarded following KfW and
ODA standard procedures (including equipment procured through UNICEF).
c. Contracts for vehicles financed by IDA, with an estimated aggregate
value of US$11.3 million, will be awarded on the basis of ICB.  In
addition, 12 vehicles (estimated total cost US$200,000) which are



- 49 -
required urgently to facilitate early project implementation will be
procured using local or international shopping. Vehicles financed by
KfW and ODA, with an estimated total value of US$0.1 million
equivalent, will be awarded following KfW and ODA standard procedures.
A domestic preference of 151, or the applicable duty, whichever is less, will be
given for locally manufactured items in evaluating bids for goods under ICB.
IDA's prior review will be required for individual contracts exceeding US$200,000
which will cover about 901 of the IDA financed procurement of goods in some 15
contracts; other contracts will be subject to selective post-award reviews.
142. Local and expatriate consultant services financed by IDA, expected to total
about US$2.0 million, will be contracted following Bank guidelines for the Use
of Consultants.   All technical assistance contracts financed by IDA will be
subject to IDA prior review.
143. The Pakistani local competitive bidding procedures are generally consistent
with the need for adequate competition and encouraging economy and efficiency in
the execution of the project. There are, however, a few provisions which require
strengthening, including:  (a) sufficient time for bid submission; (b) specifying
evaluation criteria to ensure that the lowest evaluated responsive bid wins; and
(c) bidding documents specifying clearly all factors to be used in bid
evaluation. Further details are given at Annex 8. Sample contracts for civil
works (local competitive bidding), goods (international competitive bidding) and
technical assistance have been prepared for each province and the federal level.
Assurances were obtained during Negotiations that procurement through local
competitive bidding will follow procedures acceptable to IDA.
