E. Disbursements. Accounts and Audits
144. Credit disbursements will finance: (a) 90% of the costs of civil works;
(b) for equipment, furniture, vehicles, medicines and supplies: 100% of foreign
expenditures of direct imports, 100% of ex-factory cost for locally manufactured
items procured through ICB, and 60% of local expenditures for other items
procured locally; (c) 100% of the cost net of taxes of local training, overseas
fellowships, technical assistance, and project coordination; and (d) 80% of total
incremental salaries up to a maximum disbursement of US$2.7 million, subsequently
60% of total incremental salaries up to a maximum cumulative disbursement of
US$4.4 million, and finally 40% of total incremental salaries up to a maximum
cumulative disbursement of US$5.4 million.
145. Disbursement against contracts for civil works and goods below US$200,000
all incremental salaries, fellowships and local training programs not undertaken
by contract, and project coordination will be made against itemized Statements
of Expenditure (SOE). Documentation supporting these SOEs will be retained by
the implementing agencies for review by IDA missions. All other expenditures
will be fully documented. In order to facilitate project implementation, the
governments will establish and maintain, under terms and conditions satisfactory
to IDA, three Special Accounts in a commercial bank: one operated by the DOH in
each province and one by the MOH. After Project Effectiveness and upon receipt
of the required withdrawal application, IDA will make initial deposits equivalent
to US$0.8 million for Balochistan, US$2.0 million for the Punjab, and US$0.2
million for the federal level. These Accounts will cover all expenditures both
local and foreign of less than US$80,000 for Balochistan, US$200,000 for the
Punjab and US$20,000 for the federal level. Expenditures above these amounts
could be submitted directly to IDA for payment.
146. The proposed IDA Credit of US$48.0 million will be disbursed over six
years, which includes an additional 12 months to accommodate unforeseen delays
in implementation (see Disbursement Schedule in Annex 9). The current country
disbursement profile is seven years, but the six year period reflects faster
disbursements expected through following a more programmatic and flexible
approach, combined with the anticipated positive effects of the SAP on the
implementation of social sector projects generally. Disbursements are expected
to be completed by June 30, 1999.
147. Accounts and Audits. The Ministry and Departments of Health will maintain
separate accounts and records for project expenditure including records of the
transactions of the Special Accounts and of disbursements made on the basis of
Statements of Expenditures (SOEs), in accordance with generally accepted
accounting standards and the July 8, 1992 "Guidelines on Financial Reporting and
Auditing of Projects financed by the World Bank" (the Accounting and Audit
Guidelines). Project Accounts and Special Account Statements will be prepared
annually for each province and the federal level to ensure a fair presentation
of the project's financial performance and position, disclosing all funds
provided by government, IDA and the cooperating donors, as well as expenditure
incurred during the year, in accordance with the formats provided in Annex 10,
Tables B and C. These financial statements will be made available for auditing



- 52 -
purposes to independent auditors acceptable to IDA, within four months of the end
of each fiscal year. Assurances were obtained during Negotiations that: (a)
independent auditors acceptable to IDA will be appointed in a timly manner to
undertake annual audits and to prepare audit reports of the Project Accounts,
Special Accounts and SOKs for each province and the federal level; and (b)
certified copies of the audited Project Accounts and the Special Account
Statements, together with the auditor's report and opinion thereon, and a
certified copy of the auditors report and opinion on SOKs will be provided to IDA
within nine months of the end of each fiscal year.
IV. ENEFITS AND RISKS
148. The project will help the governments achieve their objective of improving
the health status of the population generally but with a special emphasis on high
risk and under-privileged sections of the comaunity.  Services will be of a
higher quality, more available and of increased effectiveness.   Women will
benefit particularly through improved maternal health services, especially ante-
and post-natal care including increased availability of family planning services,
and children particularly through strengthened communicable disease control.
With present levels of facility coverage, by project completion, the health
services available to some 70-80X of the total population of the two provinces
(including 16 million women of childbearing age) will improve. Service use is
expected to increase from present low levels, with a doubling to 20-251 of
pregnant women in service catchment areas receiving some prenatal care and,
similarly, a doubling in the proportion of deliveries supervised (further
indicators are included in Annex 3).  The government will benefit from the
various institutional development components, particularly the management,
planning and technical skill enhancement activities. These activities will also
pave the way for more ambitious objectives to be met through subsequent
investments.
149.  The project faces three main risks.  The most important is the limited
implementation capacity of the provincial DOHs. This risk is addressed through
having a substantial management strengthening component, the more programmatic
approach allowing greater implementation flexibility than in a conventional
project, simplifying the project design as far as is consistent with maintaining
technical standards, incorporating experience from other similar projects, and
providing  for  technical  assistance  in  key  areas.    The  second  is  that
strengthening public health systems and particularly focusing on women's health
problems may not receive sufficient priority from a male dominated, traditionally
orientated health system, including poor implementation of the project family
planning activities due to the administrative separation between health and
population; this risk is minimized through fostering strong commitment to the
project from the senior officials concerned in project preparation, the project
reinforcing and financing the implementation of key parts of the governments'
primary health and maternal/child care policy, taking special steps to increase
the involvement of women in service delivery, the involvement of local
institutional support, and the establishment of coordination mechanisms.
Finally, as the government's normal PC-1 procedures are not well adapted to the



- 53 -
sectoral approach of this operation, there is the risk that those procedures will
interfere with proper implementation of the project; this risk has been minimized
by the inclusion of the maximum flexibility permitted by government rules in the
PC-is for this project, and by the requirement of annual reviews which will
induce changes in the PC-ls when needed.
V. A_R_NTS REACD AND RCI               ions
150. During Negotiations, the Federal and Provincial Governments as appropriate
provided assurances that:
a. an official statement of health sector policy, strategy and priorities
will be prepared for the two provinces, discussed with IDA and issued
by December 1993 (para. 57);
b. by March 31 of each year each province, with IDA and the cooperating
donors, will: (a) review the program and project implementation for
the past fiscal year; (b) review and agree the schedules and planned
development budgetary allocations for the program and sub-program
implementation for the coming fiscal year; and (c) agree planned
overall recurrent budgetary allocations which will include this IDA
project and other assistance for the coming fiscal year.   These
agreements will be reflected in the budget proposal for each fiscal
year. Assurances were also obtained that each province will provide
sufficient development and recurrent funding to primary and rural
health services throughout the program; that the recurrent health
budget proposal will reflect the real rate of growth of GDP; and that
preference for the allocation of additional recurrent resources will be
given to basic health services and non-salary items.  The federal
government, IDA and the cooperating donors will also review annually
the funds required for carrying out both the provincial programs and
the federal activities (para. 60);
c. essential drugs lists will be approved by the Balochistan and Punjab
Departments of Health by July 1994 and introduced by December 1994
(para. 73);
d. an in-depth study for analyzing and making recommendations on the
administrative and staffing implications of the proposed reorganization
of the communicable disease control program will be completed by July
1994, necessary measures agreed with IDA by December 1994, and
implementation started by July 1995 (para. 75);
e. in advance of the phasing of overall project implementation by
districts, about half of the government health facilities in
Balochistan and the Punjab will offer family planning services by
December 1994 and all such facilities will do so by July 1996 (para.
77);



- 54 -
f. the provincial staff development teams, which will be responsible for
developing the provincial in-service training programs, will be
established and adequately staffed by December 1993 (para. 87);
g. a study of the financial and non-financial incentives and service
conditions of female paramedical staff, both during training and
subsequent employment, will be conducted for each province by July
1994, necessary measures agreed with IDA and implementation started by
December 1994. These reviews will take account of the similar work in
progress under the Family Health project in NWFP and Sindh (para. 91);
h. (a) at least 75 percent of the professional staff of the Provincial
Health Development Centres will be recruited or transferred in by the
end of December 1993 with the Centres being fully staffed by December
1994; and (b) that at least 75 percent of the professional staff for
the District Health Development Centres will be recruited or
transferred in by the end of the first year in which they are scheduled
to begin operations with the balance appointed during the second year
of operation (para. 95);
i. (a) powers will be delegated to District Health Officers by June 1994
to hire, transfer and promote staff up to Basic Pay Scale 12 in their
districts, and (b) existing levels of financial delegation for
operating expenditures will be implemented (para. 100);
J. (a) arrangements with local universities and non-governmental
organizations for obtaining technical support for the project by the
provinces will begin during the first project year and continue
throughout the project; (b) agreements for the provision of the main
technical  assistance  consultants  for  management  and  training
development services will be signed by March 1994; and (c) IDA-financed
consultants will be recruited in accordance with Bank Guidelines dated
August 1981 (para. 134);
k. all remaining sites will be acquired and available by June 1995 (para.
137);
1. (a) semi-annual progress reports will be prepared by the Departments of
Health and submitted to IDA; (b) a joint government and IDA mid-term
review will be held before May 1996; and (c) a Project Completion
Report will be submitted by the governments to IDA within six months of
the closing date of the project (para. 138);
m. procurement through local competitive bidding will follow procedures
acceptable to IDA (para. 143); and
n. (a) independent auditors acceptable to IDA will be appointed in a
timely manner to undertake annual audits and to prepare audit reports
of the Project Accounts, Special Accounts and SOEs for each province
and the federal level; and (b) certified copies of the audited Project
Accounts and the Special Account Statements, together with the



- 55 -
auditor's report and opinion thereon, and a certified copy of the
auditors report and opinion on SOEs will be provided to IDA within nine
months of the end of each fiscal year (para. 147).
151. There is one special Condition of Credit Effectiveness: the approval of
the project PC-ls by the Executive Committee of the Federal Government's National
Economic Council (para. 139).
152.     Subject to the above conditions, the project provides a suitable basis
for an IDA Credit of SDR 35.0 million equivalent to the Government of the Islamic
Republic of Pakistan.

SECOND FAMILY HEALTH PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Health Staff
Doctors
1.        About 108,000 staff are employed in the health sector of whom some
14,000 and 20,000 are doctors employed in the public and private sectors
respectively  (1989).   Primarily to alleviate unemployment of new medical
graduates, the government has created about 2,000 jobs for junior doctors in the
public sector since 1985, but there are still estimated to be some 6,000
unemployed doctors (1989 unofficial figure). While the output from the medical
colleges has been somewhat reduced, it is still substantially in excess of
anticipated vacancies particularly for sale graduates.   Despite two major
government inquiries into the problem (1976 and 1982), no solution has been
found. Apart from the financial implications, continuing government attempts to
find public sector jobs for medical graduates is resulting in their being posted
to positions for which they have not been properly trained and to facilities not
designed to support them and thereby negatively affecting the functioning of the
primary health system.  Reform of medical curricula has yet to be seriously
started. In contrast, there is a major shortage of almost all types of 5edical
nRePialis..t  (surgeons, anesthetists, gynecologists, etc.).  This affects the
quality and availability of tertiary care offered, and also basic medical and
specialist training.
Nursing and garamedical staff
2.        Nurses are almost entirely a female cadre with some 5,800 employed in
the public sector and over 2,000 in the private sector (1989). They are usually
posted in the large urban hospitals, with most nursing posts in the smaller
hospitals being vacant.  The profession has a poor image and low status and
consequently has had great difficulty in recruiting and retaining young nurses.
Efforts during the 1980s to improve nursing salaries and allowances have had some
effect but there remains a chronic shortage of nurses throughout the country.
Current health policy and plans include proposals for major further
salary/grading/career improvements, the introduction of local training schools,
and the lowering of various entry and personal requirements.
3.        Paramedical staff cover a wide range of cadres including (a) Lady
Health Visitors (LHV), a long established female group who are the backbone of
the primary care system and who are particularly important for MCH care;
(b) Health Technicians (HT), a mixed male and female group created in the 1970s
to be posted to BHUs and RHCs; (c) technical staff, including such groups as



- 77 -
Annex 2
Page 2 of 2
laboratory technicians, operating theatre technicians and x-ray technicians, who
are found in all facilities from RHCs upwards but whose training, until recently,
has been very neglected; (d) MPW female staff, especially Family Welfare Workers,
whose training is similar to LHVs; (e) others, include dispensers a long standing
and large male group whose current position in the primary care system is vague,
vaccinators, sanitary inspectors, malaria supervisors and midwives. Details of
the numbers in each cadre are given in Annex 1. All of the female cadres are in
short supply, as are many of the male groups, although the position varies
significantly between provinces. Various proposals are included in the current
health programs to address these problems which are similar to those proposed for
nurses. A special case is the existing Female Health Technicians (FHT) who will
receive six months training in midwifery, phased in, to bring their level
approximately equal to that of the Lady Health Visitor. Also, the pre-service
training for FHTs will be extended from the present 18 months to 24 months to
include six months of midwifery.  Training quality for many of these groups is
broadly acceptable, but significant gaps remain. One problem, yet to be tackled,
is to revise paramedical training curricula/staffing to account for the advent
of doctors at peripheral health facilities. In-service training consists only
of ad hoc short courses usually linked with vertical programs; planned and
comprehensive continuing education is non-existent. The training of the trainers
of many of the paramedical cadres has often been overlooked and some of the
facilities and much of the equipment are in poor condition. The private modern
sector's demands on the paramedical groups in short supply are not great, with
the exception of nurses.

PAKISTAN
SECOND FAMILY HEALTH PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Two project frameworks follow.  The first *ulmmrizeo the relationship
between measures included in the Social Action Program (SAP) and the content of
the proposed FHII project.   The first column, headed "Objective/Strategy",
considers each of the eleven priority action points identified in the
government's SAP document of April 19921.  These points are then linked, as
appropriate, to the interventions included in the project. In turn, the proposed
interventions are related to the expected outputs for Balochistan and the Punjab
-- which are planned outputs from their development programs, of which the
project is an integral part.
The second framework follows the same structure but shows in detail how the
various priority sub-programs supported by the project in Balochistan and the
Punjab contribute to the planned objectives and expected outputs of the
provincial development programs.
pI Supplemented as necessary by policy related information in the PFP and PSAL.

PAKISTAN
SECOND FAILY HEALTH PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Strengthening Primary Health Services at the District and Periphery Level
&tM'
1.        This component aims at: (a) reducing women's mortality and morbidity;
(b) integrating contraceptive services into all health facilities; and (c)
improving the effectiveness of activities against communicable diseases. These
goals were chosen among many alternatives because they have the potential for
large gains in improving mortality and morbidity in Pakistan and are relatively
less complex to implement.   During implementation and training, particular
attention will be given to local conditions which warrant refining the focus of
these activities. The project will support a package of interventions which will
be phased-in, district by district, and when integrated with existing basic
health services, will provide more comprehensive health services to the most
vulnerable groups in Balochistan and the Punjab.
Stratei&&
2.        To reach these goals,  three main strategies will be used:   (a)
improving access to health services, in particular for women; (b) improving the
quality of health services at the district and periphery level;  and  (c)
increasing utilization of health services.
3.        The interventions required to lower maternal mortality and morbidity
and those needed to integrate family planning education and services into health
care are closely interrelated and are presented together. They include:
(a)     improved access to basic services through
(i)      expanding   Traditional   Birth   Attendant   (TBA)
training;
(ii)     experimenting with and introducing Community Health
Workers (CHW) in the Punjab;
(iii)    posting additional female staff at BHUs, RHCs and
tehsil hospitals;



- 88 -
Annex 4
Page 2 of 11
(iv)     strengthening  the referral  system  and enhancing
emergency handling capacity;
(v)      expanding NGO activities.
(b)     improved gualitv of prenatal, postnatal and delivery services
at all levels through:
(i)      in-service training for field staff (see Staff
Development component);
(ii)     upgrading  selected  BHUs  and  MCH  Centres  and
improving the availability of essential medicines
and supplies;
(iii)    enhancing nutrition activities as part of maternal
and child health (MCH) services;
(iv)     reorganizing Communicable Disease Control (CDC) and
strengthening diagnostic capacities;
(v)      improved availability of contraceptives and basic
obstetrical services in all health outlets; and
(vi)     increasing the management and supervision capability
(see Management component);
c)      Imuroved utilization of services through:
i)       strengthening health education;
(ii)     encouraging community involvement; and
(iii)    assessing service impact.
d)      I  roved urban health by undertaking similar activities in
selected urban slums.
4.        The Project will support an eansion of the ez
qKguQ   and a strengthening of the link between trained TBAs and primary health
care services. A large number of TBAs have already been trained with assistance
from UNICEF and others but lack of follow-up and linkage with the local BHU/RHC
has weakened their performance. The project will strengthen the curriculum and
training plan, and implement training activities; the BHU/RHC will become
responsible for the training and monitoring/support of TBAs in its catchment
area. In Balochistan where relatively few TBAs have been trained compared to
the Punjab, there will be emphasis on initial training whereas in the Punjab the
emphasis will be on refresher training.  Due to the difficult logistics in



- 89 -
Annex 4
Page 3 of 11
Balochistan, training and supervision will continue to be carried out by mobile
teams.  Each team is expected to train around 100 TBAs per year.  There are
already four mobile teams, each composed of a Lady Health Visitor (LHV), an ayah
and a driver.   The project will provide four more vehicles and UNICEF will
provide another four, a total of 12 mobile teams. The project will provide funds
for travel allowances and accommodation for the teams, for POL/maintenance of the
vehicles and for TBA kits.   In the Punjab, some 22,000 TBAs have been trained
and the focus will be on refresher courses to keep TBAs updated; in addition
2,600 TBAs will  receive  initial  training.    TBAs'  interventions will be
implemented during the prenatal, delivery and postpartum periods. During the
prenatal period, a trained TBA, with educative support from the BHU staff, will
attempt to have contacts with as many pregnant women as feasible. During these
contacts, the TBA will provide health education on nutrition and pregnancy, iron
and folic acid supplements and refer the pregnant women to the nearest health
facility (MCH Centre, BHU or Satellite Clinic) for tetanus immunization. Through
pictorials and other simple screening instruments, she will screen for high risk
pregnancies; all high risk pregnancies will be referred to and encouraged to
visit the BHU/RHC. A similar prenatal package will be provided at the BHU, MCH
Centre, dispensary, RHC and tehsil hospital levels with a degree of
specialization commensurate to their level of care, e.g. at the BHU a decision
on whether a woman can be delivered at home or in a health outlet will be made;
complicated pregnancies will be hospitalized at the RHC or tehsil hospital. The
trained TBA will provide a clean supervised delivery at home to the low risk
pregnant women, referring emergent complications to the nearest health unit.
After home delivery, the TBA will reemphasize breastfeeding, postpartum and
infant care,and the availability and benefits of contraceptives. The LHV will
supervise the TBAs in the villages around her BHU/RHC, she will replace
consumables and build a link between the TBAs and the local health facility. The
project will complement resources provided by UNICEF for new training, refresher
training, supervisory costs and consumable supplies. To facilitate the permanent
link between these self-supporting village practitioners and the health system,
the BHU/RHC will provide them with continuing education, basic drugs, resupply
for delivery kits as well as monitoring their referral pattern and record
keeping. Twenty-four hour coverage for delivery services will be offered at the
BHU or MCH Centre by female health staff, midwife, or trained TBA. Postpartus
care will be provided by the TBA through two home visits in the Punjab (24 hours
and four to six weeks after delivery) and one referral to the BHU; in
Balochistan, the target will be less ambitious due to the difficult logistics.
Home visits will focus on encouraging universal breastfeeding, monitoring
bleeding and fever in the mother. Information and education on infant health,
breastfeeding, nutrition, postpartum complications and family planning will be
reemphasized. Contraception will be promoted to start early after delivery, or
earlier in absence of breastfeeding (infant death).   Trained TBAs will be
encouraged to distribute or sell oral rehydration salts (ORS), iron & folic acid,
acetaminophen, pills and condoms.
, ,  ,>.   .
5.        Although the establishment of CMfl  was included in the 1990 health
policy, some aspects of the sustainability of the cadre have yet to be fully



- 90 -
Annex 4
Page 4 of 11
developed, including how they would be remunerated. For the Puniab only, the
project will define the task descriptions of the male and female CHW, update the
training curriculum and plan and implement their training. The role of the CHWs
will be to increase community awareness of health problems, emphasize prevention
and provide minimum basic curative care. They will be trained and supervised by
health workers from the local RHC/BHU, and they will receive a kit containing
essential drugs as well as contraceptives.  The project will provide all the
training inputs for both trainers and CHWs.   Based on a review of existing
experience, a CHW training curriculum and materials, guidelines for recruitment,
sustainability and implementation strategies will be developed. The CHW will be
selected by their fellow villagers according to criteria such as age, literacy,
and commitment to serve the community voluntarily. There is, however, a need to
sustain these volunteers in money or in kind in order to maintain their
commitment. One possibility among others which will be tried is the selling of
drugs by the CHWs in order to create a revolving fund which will be used to
remunerate them and to buy replacements. In order to avoid problems such as lack
of accountability, overcharging and overprescribing which can be experienced in
such revolving funds, simple ways will be tested to monitor abuses or mishandling
of the drug kits. If successful, this system would be a way to rationalize and
monitor drug consumption, besides offering essential drugs at lower prices than
the open market.
6.        Additional staff.   The achievement of the project objectives is
dependent upon the availability of health workers, especially females. Human
resources in the health sector are characterized by shortage of female workers
especially in Balochistan. Increasing the number of female health care workers
will be achieved through a series of interventions which are described in
Annex 5. In Balochistan, the project will include the salaries of 90 FHT/LHVs,
while in both provinces it will support incremental transport and minor equipment
costs of female paramedical staff for increased outreach activities.
7.         Strengthenine the referral aXstem and emergencv handling capacitl will
increase the credibility of the health system. In Balochistan, some 36 district
hospitals and RHCs will be provided with a labor room and about 22
district/tehsil hospitals will serve as first referral services for obstetric
emergencies.    They will be  equipped  to handle  intravenous  (i.v.)  fluid
rehydration, i.v. antibiotic and oxygen therapy for infants, forceps delivery,
vacuum extraction and Caesarean Section besides offering the capability to
diagnose and stabilize complex obstetric and pediatric problems. The project
will support the costs of equipment, incremental drugs and replenishable items.
Such centers must have a female Medical Officer (MO) with internship training in
Obstetrics-Gynecology and six months additional Obstetrics training and a male
MO with two months training in anaesthesia. Given the projected schedule for
training it is anticipated that 50 Female MOs and 100 Male MOs will receive such
training. There will be six divisional second referral centers with labor rooms,
a functioning radiology unit; four of these will have a blood bank.   These
secondary referral units will manage major emergencies as well as the more
complicated obstetrical and pediatric cases. Such centers will have functioning



- 91 -
Annex 4
Page 5 of 11
labor rooms. It is anticipated that each of these centers will have a surgeon/
gynecologist and anesthesiologist in addition to the MO team described in the
first level referral centers. Project support will include equipment for blood
banks, radiology units and anesthesia and 12 equipped ambulances with drivers.
Experiments will be conducted on alternatives to conventional emergency transport
and communications.   In the Punjab, many RHCs already have a female MO with
Obstetrics training and it will be easier than in Balochistan to strengthen the
referral system.  At the end of the project the first referral centers will
include 200 RHCs with walking blood bank, labor suites, radiology units and
laboratories, and 60 tehsil hospitals with walking blood banks, ultrasound
machines and incubators. Thirty district hospitals will function as secondary
referral centres with a blood bank or a walking blood bank, a functioning
radiology unit, an ultrasound machine, and a functioning labor room. The type
of staff in the referral centers are the same as described for Balochistan. A
functioning  communication  (radios/telephones)  and  transport  system  (30
ambulances) will link the peripheral to the referral units. In both Provinces
MOs will have additional training in emergency procedures, especially related to
obstetric emergencies.
8.        Selected health units, from dispensaries to hospitals, are run by a
variety of NGQ0. The provincial Health Departments are interested in expanding
their support and cooperation with the NGOs in order to increase health care to
people below the poverty line - the area of NGO interest. The activities of the
NGOs to be financed by the project are consistent with the above project
subcomponents, but the mode of financing will be different in the two provinces.
The Balochistan Government will channel funds to the NGOs as grants through
standard government procedures. The Punjab Government will channel funds to NGOs
through the Punjab Health Foundation (HF).  In Balochistan, the project will
support the upgrading of equipment of a female hospital, renovation of MCH
facilities belonging to three NGOs and the establishment of two 25-bed maternity
hospitals of the Family Planning Association of Pakistan (FPAP). The project
will also support an experimental training and deployment of voluntary community
health workers (CHW) by FPAP.   In the Punjab, the project will support the
construction of two maternity hospitals, construction and upgrading of health
units and support two PHC rural models through NGOs.
9.        BRUNCH Centras will be uptraded and their equipment brought upto
standard. In selected areas where BHUs are not readily accessible, satellite
clinics will be established. These satellite clinics will be established in an
existing house in the village which will be provided free of charge to the
Department of Health. They will be staffed by a LHV from the BHU on days and
times agreed upon in consultation with the villagers. Travel allowances will be
provided to the LHV for attending the satellite clinic. The project will provide
an examining table, basic furniture, equipment (midwife and IUD kit), and
essential drugs. In Balochistan, where facilities are less available than in the
Punjab, some 30 dispensaries will be upgraded to offer MCH services like MCH
Centres, equipped, furnished and staffed, and accommodation for LHVs provided.
About 48 MCH Centres will be provided with LHV accommodation. In the Puniab, the



- 92 -
Annex 4
Page 6 of 11
project will provide MCH baby resuscitation and weighing equipment to 2,300 BHUs
plus where necessary, BHUs will receive furniture, IUD tables and other
equipment. 101 satellite clinics will be equipped and furnished. A consultation
system via telephone or radio will be organized with a transport system allowing
transfer of emergencies between levels of care; and supervision, management and
training will be facilitated by the District Health Development Center (see annex
5).
10        ImDroved availabilitl  of essential sedicines and sullies.   PHC
facilities need large quantities of a small number of drugs but in practice are
stocked with a wide variety of drugs, many of which are not essential.  The
Medical Store Depots (MSD) have a list of basic drugs which should form the basis
of purchase orders every year. An Essential Drugs List (EDL) has been approved
in the Punjab and is under study in Balochistan. The implementation of the EDL
for different categories of PHC facilities is essential in order to eliminate
purchase of irrational drugs and standardize drug prescription. Another problem
is the thousands of private drug stores run by unqualified persons in violation
of sale rules. There is no immediate solution to this problem except to improve
the skills of untrained persons working at the private pharmacies through short-
term courses.   The project aims at a) implementing an EDL; b) developing
procedures and guidelines for decentralization of supplies and verification of
purchase of drugs at the divisional level including sample checking for quality
on a pilot basis; c) providing untrained persons working in private pharmacies
with short-term training courses to improve their skills; d) assessment of
existing budget allocations for drugs for the PHC outlets in the light of the
EDL. In Balochistan, the project will provide funds for incremental essential
drugs to 545 BHU/MCH Centres, 75 dispensaries, 55 RHCs and 52 first line referral
centers. Funds will be provided for three trucks and for salaries and training
of incremental staff for the MSD. In the Punjab, the project will provide funds
for incremental essential drugs to 1,600 BHU/MCH Centres, finance a study to
design the curriculum for training Pharmacy Assistants, identify potential
training places and design a training plan. A decentralized distribution system
will be implemented. In both Provinces, staff of the MSDs will be trained in
procurement, inventory control, distribution of drugs and equipment, and the
implementation of the EDL will be monitored.
11.       Family planning. At the village level, family planning education and
contraceptives (condoms and pills) will be provided by TBAs and CHWs under the
supervision and support of the BHU/RHC/MCH Centre staff who will supply similar
services as an integral part of MCH. IUD insertion will be referred to RHCs
where a lady doctor or LHV would have been suitably  trained.   Surgical
contraception will be made available at some of the first and all of the second
referral centers. The project will support:
a)      pre- and in-service training of health workers in family
planning service provision as a component of MCH. The project
will support training of MOs and paramedics as appropriate in
surgical contraception, IUD insertion and other forms of FP.



- 93 -
Annex 4
Page 7 of 11
b)      ensuring a reliable supply of equipment, contraceptives and
information materials to all health facilities and providers.
The project will provide funds for the purchase of equipment
for training, service provision including information and
counselling materials, surgical equipment and contraceptives.
In order to have a reliable supply, a monitoring and
supervision system should be in place.  The Departments of
Health (DOH) will develop a reporting system of FP indicators
in collaboration with the Population & Welfare Department.
c)      improving the capacity of the Departments of Health to manage
the family planning component of the project. In the Puniab,
the project will support an additional post at grade 19 in the
Directorate to work with the Chief Inspectress of Health in
the detailed management of family planning.   A series of
'population awareness" workshops for all the Directors of
Health Services and appropriate training in family planning
program design and evaluation for staff of the Planning Cell.
12.       Nutrition activities will be part of the MCH component and will include
growth monitoring, nutrition education, provision of iron folate to pregnant and
lactating women through TBAs and health outlets, provision of vitamin A tablets
to high risk children through the health outlets.   Integration of nutrition
edication into health education programs focusing on breastfeeding, appropriate
weaning, increased dietary intake during pregnancy and diarrhea, consumption of
vitamin A rich fruits and vegetables and foods with vitamin C for iron
absorption. In both provinces, a nutrition section will be established in the
Director General Health's Office, manned by at least one senior technical staff
to undertake development of nutrition activities. He/she will liaise with MCH
and health education specialists, pre-service training schools, and participate
in policy development which will be coordinated by the Planning and Development
Department. Each Divisional Director will monitor nutrition activities at the
divisional level and will be assisted by the Divisional Inspectress of Health.
World  Food  Programme  (WFP)  activities  will  be  integrated with  the  new
arrangements for nutrition activities with experimentation for better targeting
of WFP commodities.   Consequently,  the project includes the provision of
essential micro nutrients, scales, growth charts, and educational material to
clinics and outreach workers, as well as funds for operational research, and
limited technical staff strengthening and support. Nutrition activities will be
fully integrated with the MCH and health education subcomponents.
13.       Coumnmicable diseases are the main causes of mortality and morbidity
in Pakistan.   The project will  integrate at the periphery most vertical
Communicable Diseases Control programs whose staff will be reorganized in multi-
purpose teams. These multi-purpose workers will be based at the BHU/RHC and will
cover one Union Council. In the Punjab, a Union Council area is less than in
Balochistan and therefore the multi-purpose team will be formed by two or three
workers who will travel by bicycles. In Balochistan, where distances are longer



- 94 -
Page 8 of 11
and logistics are more difficult, the multi-purpose team will be formed by four
workers. There are two possible options to divide the workload among the four
workers within the Union Council depending on local circumstances. The first
option would be to assign one worker at the static facility, the second worker
would cover with the bicycle the villages nearby and the other two workers would
cover the rest of the area by motorcycle. The second option would be to divide
the Union Council into two halves and each part would be covered by a pair of
workers on motorcycles. This new organization combined with a closer integration
with other PHC activities, will require a major re-training effort (see Staff
Development Component).  Disease surveillance will be introduced for monitoring
and control  strategies.   The project will  finance transport,  cold chain
equipment,  consumable  supplies  and  medicines.        Major  changes  in  the
organizational set up, merger of cadres, expanded role of out-reach workers and
the concept of working in a team, besides upgrading the technical and managerial
skills of almost all the out-reach workers and the supervisors will be
undertaken. Activities will include:
i.      a disease surveillance system will be implemented through
sentinel facilities for monitoring essential communicable
diseases to assess the impact of control strategies, identify
endemic areas and improve the quality of outreach services;
ii.     functional integration of all mono-purpose outreach workers,
improve their technical skill by continuing in-service
training and mobility through supply of proper transport,
develop links with the grass root workers like TBA/CHWs;
iii.    provision of minilabs  at BHU level  to facilitate  early
diagnosis and management of cases of malaria and pulmonary
tuberculosis;
iv.     ensuring improved supply of drugs;
v.      decentralization of authority and responsibility to the RHC
level to facilitate routine management decisions at the local
level.
The project will:
(a)     improve access through:
(i)      the training of male and female CHWs (Punjab only)
who will be responsible for minor curative services
and for preventative and promotive health activities
at the village level.   The female CHW/TBA will
provide education to mothers and the community on
CDC, and participate in immunization activities with
the existing team of vaccinators; and



- 95 -
Annex 4
Page 9 of 11
(ii)     decentralization   of   tuberculosis   and  malaria
prevention to the village level by the CHW and of
laboratory and curative services to the BHU level.
Passive case findings and ambulatory treatment
(including   three   first-line   antibiotics   for
tuberculosis) will be offered at each BHU/RHC.
(b)     Improve the quality of services through:
(i)      integration    at  the  periphery  of  the vertical
programs such as Expanded Programme of Immunization
(EPI), malaria and tuberculosis into a coherent and
more cost effective primary health care system, in
order to improve the quality and cost effectiveness
of existing services for CDC. EPI will be expanded
to most BHU and MCH Centres where equipment and
supplies will be provided if needed.   The main
project inputs will be for: laboratory services with
240 minilabs in Balochistan and 1900 in the Punjab;
transport with 100 mopeds and 21 four wheel drive
(4WD) vehicles in Balochistan, 7,000 bicycles, 512
motorcycles and 113 4WD vehicles in the Punjab;
malaria control equipment will be provided in both
provinces and major replacements for cold chain
equipment will be provided to the Punjab.   The
project will finance the incremental recurrent costs
for: (i) Director of CDC and other staff in
Balochistan and for one epidemiologist and computing
equipment in the Punjab; (ii) in-service training
(see Annex 5) will be provided in TB surveillance,
laboratory    techniques,    malaria    microscopy,
epidemiology and other subjects  related to CDC.
This will upgrade medical and paramedical staff
skills and enable them to fulfill their new
responsibilities in an integrated approach;   (iii)
essential drugs and supplies, furniture and IEC
equipment will be provided on the basis of an agreed
list of essential drugs appropriate to each PHC
facility. The lack of drugs and supplies is a major
cause of under-utilization of peripheral health
facilities; (iv) a new Public Health Laboratory in
Quetta, Balochistan, will be constructed, equipped
and furnished. The project will support the costs
of one 4WD vehicles, incremental staff and staff
training.

14.       The project aims at improving service utilization by supporting:
(i)     health and nutrition education activities which will be
expanded through: a) expansion of staff so that each district
will have a health education officer; b) the provision of
basic transport, production and reproduction equipment so that
both Departments can do part of their own materials
production; c) the provision of funds for the reproduction and
distribution of materials; d) provision of funds for
contracting studies, communication and media activities to
NGOs and private organizations; and e) in-service training for
health staff in effective interpersonal health education. The
project will establish a health education capability at the
divisional or district level by the end of the project and
will integrate health education into the routine work of the
primary health care system. Most health education materials
will be supplied from the provincial level, but some supplies
and equipment will be available at the districtl;
(ii)    increased community involvement with local health services
through an information campaign which would create population
awareness on health problems especially those related to MCH.
Awareness would motivate people to take direct action to
tackle their problems through the selection and sustenance of
TBA/CHWs and in some cases the formation of village health
committees.   People would be actively involved in taking
decisions to improve their conditions and through the
Committee or the Union Council they would test the feasibility
of community financing for their BHU and other health
services. Similar experiments in community health financing
will be tested with local BHU/RHCs.   Health staff will be
trained in communication techniques in order to create
community awareness, advise on the management of resources
given by the community and take decisions with a higher degree
of autonomy from the central level.  This will be possible
with decentralization of responsibility and accountability for
management, supervision and training.
(iii)   assessment  of  the  implementation  and  impact of project
activities through studies which will assess reasons behind
impact or lack of it, quality of services, public perception
of services and other topics related to improvement of
services.   In Balochistan, the mid-term and end of project
evaluation will be supplemented by quality assurance studies
of services delivered and operational research on mobility of
1 Health education activities are partly integrated into the proposed Provincial and District Health
Developent Centres (see below).

female health workers or female patients and on home-made
weaning foods and ORS. In the Punjab, a study to assess the
most cost-effective modalities of service delivery, a mid-term
external evaluation of process indicators and an end of
project evaluation to assess impact will be conducted. These
studies will be integrated with the health system research of
the federal component (see federal component).
d) Urban PHC
15.       The majority of this subcomponent will be implemented in the city of
Lahore where the project will expand various well-integrated PHC activities which
are in place and are considered to be promising.  It will improve preventive
health services through provision of a vehicle with cold boxes to deliver
vaccines to each of the EPI and MCH Centres and provide equipment and vehicles
for malaria control.  The project will increase access to health and family
planning services by providing accommodation for LHVs at 23 MCH Centres and
constructing labor rooms at 33 MCH Centres. Quality of care will be improved
through basic obstetrics and IUD insertion training of LHVs and female MOs and
provision of contraceptives and IEC materials to the MCH Centres. The project
will provide transport for supportive and educative supervision. Equipment for
labor rooms will be provided to 63 MCH Centres and eight dispensaries will be
upgraded into fully equipped diagnostic units. The epidemiological surveillance
and operational research capacity of the Lahore Municipal Corporation (IMC) will
be strengthened.   The experience of the Lahore urban health system will be
evaluated in order to assess what works and whether it is possible to transfer
the successes to other urban areas of the Punjab. This study will be an input
to developing scientific plans for the provision of primary care services
elsewhere in urban Punjab. Additionally, small inputs such as minilabs, fridges
for vaccines and syringes will be provided in the cities of Bahavalpur and
Sarghoda in the Punjab. A study will be conducted in order to develop scientific
plans for the provision of primary care services in a variety of different types
of urban area. These plans will be implemented in Bahawalpur and Sarghoda after
the second year. In Quetta, Balochistan, the project aims to increase service
access by constructing four MCH Centres, providing two ambulances and supplying
health education materials. Quality will be improved through better coordination
of existing PHC activities, training of LHVs and female MOs in family planning
and provision of equipment for sanitation and malaria control.

number of indicators and decisions to be made are summarized in the tables in

PAITAN
SECOND FAMILY HEALTH PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
Health Staff and Management Develonment
A substantial part of the project's objectives will be realized through
quantitative and qualitative improvements in the health system's technical and
managerial staff. To achieve these changes, manpower investments, mostly in-
service but also in pre-service training, will be required. Since neither in-
service training nor management development are recognized line activities of the
provincial Health Departments, it will be necessary for the project to encourage
the institutionalization of these important activities.    To this end,  a
Provincial Health Development Centre (PHDC) will be established in Quetta,
Balochistan and Lahore, Punjab.   It will comprise four units:   management
development,   staff  development,   health   and  nutrition  education  and
research/monitoring. In addition, a modest District Health Development Centre
(DHDC) will be established in each divisional headquarters in Balochistan and in
each district headquarters in the Punjab, to assist the district teams' work and
to provide training and hostel facilities. In general, such facilities at the
district level do not presently exist.   Further details on the staffing and
functions of the PHDCs and the DHDCs are given in the main text (see the detailed
project description) and in Annex 6.
I.  Training
All Health Workers
1.        In both provinces,  the project will effect  improvements  in the
training, performance and support of all cadres of health workers through:
(a) review of curricula of pre-service and in-service education/training
by a special curricula and studies group established at the Provincial
Health Development  Centre  (PHDC).    That  group will  review  the
curricula for PHC, especially MCH, and related subjects.  Areas of
deficiency will be identified and curricula, training programs and
educational materials will be developed and tested and made available
to tutors/trainers in pre-service, as well as in-service, programs.
An educational center will be established at the PHDC to acquaint
teachers and tutors of all cadres of health workers with the
curriculum modules and educational materials.  Technical assistance
will be provided to the PHDC to carry out these activities;
(b) The development of a system for in-service training which is geared to
the learning needs of the trainees, is interactive, reinforced, and
supported during work.   Training will be organized so that each
individual will be able to reinforce and support the training of



- 99 -
Annex 5
Page 2 of 10
health workers for which she or he is responsible. In each district
in the Punjab and in each division in Balochistan the staff of each
District Health Development Centre (DHDC) will consist of one senior
medical officer, one health/nutrition education officer, training
officers and support staff.   In the Punjab there will be also one
monitoring/evaluation officer at the DHDC. They will work with the
health workers within the district, assist in assessing learning
needs, contribute to preparing curricula and training programs,
implement training, and evaluate outcomes through assessment of job
performance. In turn, this cadre of district trainers will have been
trained and supported by a provincial group of master trainers located
at the PHDC.  Training facilities will be provided at the district
level.
In the first and part of the second project year, a cadre of potential
master trainers will be given intensive training by experts.   The
experts will be both local and expatriate and will eventually become
the counterparts  of the master trainers.   The master trainers,
supported by their counterparts, will conduct a workshop to train a
cadre of trainers from the districts, refresher training will be
conducted annually. The master trainers will be conducting training
sessions for district trainers until each district has a small group
of trainers.
Following their initial training, the district trainers will conduct
in-service training workshops for various cadres of health workers.
The district trainers will be supported by the master trainers for
these activities.   Every year, the district trainers will receive
training to refresh and further develop their skills.
Information generated through assessment of learning needs of health
workers and the impact of in-service training will be conveyed to the
PHDC curricula studies group and then to those responsible for pre-
service programs.
(c) Initiating in-service training to ensure that all cadres have adequate
knowledge and skills for allotted jobs - the focal group will be the
cadres of workers employed at the BHU, RHC, and tehsil hospitals.
(d) The strengthening of MCH services and integration of vertical programs
will result in changed roles and responsibilities of many health
workers.  The Staff Development Unit at the PHDC will have overall
responsibility for the revision and development of job descriptions
that reflect job expectations.
(e) Integration of in-service training in the areas of nutrition, health
education and communicable disease control within the general in-
service training program for all cadres of health workers.   The
objective is that all health workers perceive knowledge and skills
relative to these areas as integral to carrying out their job
responsibilities.



- 100 -
Annex 5
Page 3 of 10
(f) Addressing the training of adequate numbers of female health workers
and their recruitment and retention in posts in rural areas through
conducting social studies on recruitment, retention in training
programs and career posts, improving strategies for increasing the
number of female health workers available, and occupying service posts
particularly in the rural areas.   In Balochistan, a Recruitment
Coordinator post will be designated at the PHDC. The Coordinator will
develop and implement a recruitment plan, liaise with the principals
of the female health and family welfare training schools, liaise with
the other members of the PHDC and coordinate recruitment efforts of
girls for paramedical schools. There will be two mobile recruitment
teams staffed with a full-time LHV, FHT, Nurse and a driver. They
will visit girls and teachers in high schools, and girls' parents and
community leaders to inform them about training and career
opportunities of health workers.   They will encourage teachers and
health workers to participate in the recruitment drive.  A Student
Health Education (SHE) program will be developed at the PHDC and
introduced to provide health education to girls in their senior school
years, and to introduce girls to the work of females in the health
professions. A Parent Education Program (PEP) will be undertaken to
aid parents overcome the stigma they attach to having their daughters
enroll  in  careers  as  female  health  workers.         Additionally,
administrative steps will be taken to relax training school admission
policies and dates.  During training, stipends will be increased and
transport allowances provided. In order to improve staff retention in
rural areas, various approaches will be tested including: (a) bonus
after completion of bond period, one advance increment on serving one
year after the bond and two advance increments on serving three years
after the bond period; ; (b) hard areas incentives; and (c) award of
certificate for length of service. In the Punjab, where the problems
are less difficult, the approaches will include: (a) allow non-
residential pre-service training; and (b) relax admission criteria for
pre-service training.   To increase staff retention in rural areas,
approaches to be tried include: (a) establish policies on posting
serving couples to the same station; (b) liberalize policy of leave of
absence (leave without pay) up to five years; (c) female staff should
have preference for housing in rural health facilities; (d) allow more
pay for night duty; and (e) hard areas allowances.
Pre-service training
2.        The Second Family Health Project will be implemented in parallel with
the proposed Health Care Development Project of the Asian Development Bank (ADB).
For female paramedical staff in Balochistan, the ADB project is proposing to
finance three Public Health Schools, two Nursing Schools, one paramedical
technician school and a (male) Health Technician School. These would meet the
province's requirements for new training facilities for the next decade. The
Second Family Health Project will provide small inputs to strengthen existing
institutions including the Quetta Public Health School through provision for
additional teaching and support staff, one vehicle, a student bus for transport
to field sites, minor renovations, and a fully equipped labor room. The quality
of the training at the Sandeman School of Nursing at Quetta is adversely affected
by the lack of qualified tutors. Support under this project to the school will



- 101 -
Annex 5
Page 4 of 10
consist of provision of additional teaching and support staff and student
transport. Existing Female Health Technicians will receive a six month course
in midwifery in order to bring their level approximately equal to that of the
Lady Health Visitors.    Also  the pre-service  training  for  Female Health
Technicians (FHT) will be extended from the present 18 months to 24 months to
include six months of midwifery. These needs will be met through the expansion
of the two Female Health Technician (FHT) Schools at Quetta and Khuzdar which
will be used for both pre and in-service midwifery training. Once the in-service
training is complete, the facilities will be used to expand the pre-service
training capacity. Support to be provided under this project to each FHT School
will consist of (a) constructing, equipping and furnishing of a hostel for 40
students, an MCH Centre and accommodation for additional teaching staff; (b)
construction of an additional classroom and three offices at each school;(c)
additional teaching resources; (d) three vehicles for transporting of students
to field sites and for domiciliary midwifery services; and (e) additional
teaching and support staff. The Controller, Nursing Examination Board will be
provided with one vehicle and office equipment. At the Department of Community
Medicine of the Bolan Medical College in Quetta, a Health Education Cell will be
established and BHUs near the College will be strengthened for use as training
sites.
3.        In the Puniab, for female staff the ADB Project proposes to upgrade 10
Nursing Schools, one Post-graduate College of Nursing, one Dental Technician
School and establish 18 small Nursing Schools, two Public Health Schools, one
Paramedical College and one Dental Paramedical School.   In order to provide
midwifery training to FHTs and to increase the output of LHVs the Second Family
Health Project will provide for the construction of a new school building, hostel
for 100 students, staff residences, incremental staff, equipment and transport
at the existing Public Health Schools at Lahore and Multan.  Construction of
schools, hostels, residences, furniture, equipment, transport and incremental
staff for two new Public Health Schools at Chakwal and Sahiwal will also be
provided. The College of Community Medicine in Lahore is the main institution
in Pakistan providing training in public health.   It has a critical role in
preparing staff for leadership roles in public health and in the development of
an effective and efficient PHC system. The project will provide assistance in
the form of two full-time senior expatriate faculty members who will assist the
Principal in developing a strategy for the institution. They will assist in
elaborating research proposals and contribute to the development of the permanent
staff.   Fellowships for full-time study for two years abroad to prepare new
faculty members will be provided as a means of developing a critical mass of
qualified staff on a long term basis. The Project will also provide transport
for student field work.
In-service training
4.        In-service training will be geared to the learning needs of the
workers, interactive, reinforced and supported in the work environment.  It will
be integrated with supervision and provided as close as possible to the work-
place.   In-service training will ensure that the health workers have the
necessary knowledge and skills to perform roles associated with the objectives
of this project based on the primary health care concept. There is a need to
have a cadre of master trainers and trainers who will work with specific cadres
of workers, assist in determining learning needs, prepare training courses and



- 102 -
Annex 5
Page 5 of 10
materials, implement training and evaluate outcomes through job performance. All
trainers, including the master trainers will be given refresher courses on a
regular basis. All in-service training will follow a similar format, that is
initial training followed by regular refresher training.    The Health Human
Resources Development Cell of the Provincial Health Development Centre (PHDC)
will have overall responsibility for the development of learning resources as
well as for the training itself.
5.        In Balochistan, the project support for in-service training includes:
(a) MCH/FP:

requisite condition for acceptance into the program.

complications of childbirth, minilaparatomy, low forceps delivery and vacuum
extraction at Sandeman Hospital;
- 21 Assistant Inspectresses of Health Services will receive training in MCH,
management and supervision;
- training in MCH/Nutrition will be provided to 300 LHV/FHTs;
- training in family planning (FP) will be given to 30 nurses; and
(b) CDC:

be given at the TB Centres and will be provided by the staff of the Centres

lab staff.

(b) CDC:

district level will receive training in supervisory techniques and communication
skills;

control and prevention and communication skills in health and nutrition
education;

7.        In both provinces the project will support the costs of tutor training,

II. Organization and Management
8.        The aims of the Organization and Management component are to strengthen
the provincial health systems through:
(a) increasing managerial capacity and improving managerial and
supervisory performance at the provincial and district levels
primarily through training;
(b) improving provincial and district level managerial systems and
procedures;
(c) improving and making operational a management and supervisory system
for primary health care facilities at provincial and district levels;
(d) developing an expanded and better integrated monitoring, evaluation,
financial and operations research capacity primarily at the provincial
level and later at the district level;
(e) increasing resources for management and supervisory functions, at the
provincial and district levels; and
(f) furthering decentralization of real decision making authority as well
as beginning involving others outside the public sector health
infrastructure in health management decision making in a more
structured objective manner.
9.        The  blending  of  these  interventions  with  technical  elements,
particularly nutrition and health education, is designed to help integrate such
activities into the staff's normal tasks in the same manner as improved
management and supervision.    Further,  the basic  integration strategy for
management performance improvement is in-service training reinforced by
supportive supervision. This is the saoe strategy as for nutrition and health
education.   Therefore, linking these with management development activities
should assist in ensuring they are more effectively integrated into PHC staff
tasks.
Each of these sub-components are elaborated below:
(A)  Increasing caRacity and imRroving Rerformance of managerial staff
10.       The increases in managerial capacity at the provincial and district
levels will come through the PHDC and DHDCs. The DHDCs will require incremental



- 104 -
Annex 5
Page 7 of 10
staff for the paramedical trainers and health education staff. The improvement
in managerial job performance at all levels will come primarily through in-
service training. There will be a variety of courses which will be developed for
managers at the various levels of health system operations.
(a) Provincial level
In-service Management Development Workshops
11.       The staff of the PHDC will conduct internal workshops to develop the
workshops that they will deliver to the district staff. The initial workshops
will be about three weeks in duration and will be conducted by the external
technical assistance staff. There will be one for PHC management and another for
hospital management.   These will be followed by a one-week course revision
workshop, probably at the beginning of the second year of the project. There
will then be a curriculum development workshop of two weeks every year
thereafter, in order to update the content and revise training methodologies.
Orientation and Rlanning course for health administrators
12.       This is an important activity which will serve as an in-service
educational program for administrators. It will include the following:
-    provincial level health planning;
-    provincial level health operations management;
-    provincial level monitoring and evaluation;
-    providing  information  about  and  discussing  local  health  care
development and research activities;
-    assisting the administrators in keeping them informed about national
and international developments in the field; and
-    initiating a planning process for administrative improvements.
13.       The  participants  are  expected  to be  all  the  key  Secretariat,
Directorate and Divisional managers, including those who are special project
directors and those in charge of health planning in the Planning and Development
Department. Materials from these workshops will also be used to develop the
provincial level health manager's handbook.
(b) District and Tehsil Level Institutional Management. Staff DeveloRment
and Supervision. and PHC Technical Training
14.       All district level health service managerial staff and medical officers
serving in PHC facilities will undergo technical primary health care training
followed by refresher courses.  This will be needed for the duration of the
project as medical officers have had little or no public health training as part
of their pre-service education. It will also serve to keep up with technological
developments in public health practice and care.
15.       The same group of staff will also be trained in managesent. sunervision
d community relations.  The project's objective of obtaining higher levels of
service utilization will depend on the capacity of managers to build trust and
obtain active cooperation from the communities. This requires particular skills,
leadership and commitment to coimunity involvement. A key task of managers is



- 105 -
Annex 5
Page 8 of 10
to bring the services and the communities together.   Therefore, during this
training, there will be specific exercises for managers to practice managing
community relationships.
(c) Nutrition Management
16.       The main aspects of the nutrition program in the project will be:
(a)  Better  nutritional  practices.    To provide  women,  families  and
communities with sound knowledge of good health and nutrition
practices, with particular emphasis on management of early and
exclusive breast-feeding, timely introduction of supplementary foods,
and the nutrition of pregnant and lactating women;
(b)  Growth monitoring and promotion, coupled with the above;
(c)  Hicronutrients.  For anemia to make iron - folate available in pre-
natal and post-natal clinics, and Vitamin A where needed.
17.       A Nutrition Unit as part of the PHDC will be established.   The
functions of the unit will emphasize:   (i) gathering and making available
existing knowledge on nutrition; (ii) assisting in preparing nutrition policy;
and (iii) making such policy operational through various programmatic
interventions. The staffing of the provincial Nutrition Unit will include a
physician, one nutrition educator, one media research officer, one design
officer, one projectionist and one media specialist.
18.       Orientation and training are essential for all those involved in the
promotion and implementation of nutrition activities. Nutrition education and
demonstration procedures will be integrated into the technical in-service
training.  After health staff have integrated nutrition education into their
tasks, some orientation will be given to members of Union Councils and leading
community members through interpersonal communication.
(B) ImDroving Provincial and District Level Managerial Systems
19.       One of the problems with improving management in health systems is that
the job tasks and the related operational procedures are unclear.  This sub-
component will develop managers' manuals for each major type of managerial
position at the provincial, district, and tehsil levels. These manuals will take
each item of the job description and define exactly how it is to be carried out
and provide samples of all the relevant planning, requisition, monitoring and
reporting forms as well as other official instructions. In this process, out of-
date procedures and systems will be identified and modified as necessary. These
manuals will also serve as a basis for training. This activity will be intensive
for the first three to four years of the project, but maintained as an ongoing
function as systems and procedures are ever-changing.
(C)  ImDroving Management and Supervisory System for PHC
20.       The project will facilitate management and supervision by the DHOs'
office.   In particular,  the field supervisory tasks of the ADHO's will be
supported and regularized.   The ADHOs will visit each PHC facility in the



- 106 -
Armex 5
Page 9 of 10
subdivision/tehsil at least once a month and review, assist and plan its work.
These visits will feed information into the Management Information System (MIS)
and in turn supply analyzed information back to health facilities.
21.       The district team will produce an annual District Health Development
Plan, which will be an aggregation of local plans for every facility. The plans
will start simply with only one or two items being planned; for example, drug
supply and/or equipment maintenance. The experience of this regularized planning
and supervision will be linked to the in-service training so that they mutually
reinforce changes in performance.
(D) Developing. Monitoring and Operations Research CaRacity
22.       The capacity for program monitoring is being built up, but needs rapid
expansion if it is to provide a basis for improved data-based decision making.
Capacity for operational research will also be built up to address strategic
questions for health care development, not answerable through available
information.   The first steps for operations research will be taken by the
Research, Evaluation and Monitoring Unit of the PHDC.   They will define the
research needs with the provincial and district managers. Early in the project,
operations research will be a subject covered in an action planning workshop
which should reach agreement on three or four research priorities. These should
then be planned by the staff of the Unit and carried out by a research firm, NGO,
University or similar organization on a contract basis.   Assistance will be
available through the research component of the Federal Ministry of Health.
(E) Increasing Resources for Management Functions
InRrovement of the Management and Financial Information System Design and
OReration
23.       The current management information system collects a considerable
amount of information, but much of it never leaves the facilities or the
district.  In addition, facility and district managers are not taught how to
aggregate, analyze, and use available information in their planning or
operational decision making. Therefore, little use is made even of available
information.
24.       For the provincial level and below, the Child Survival Project has
developed a PHC-oriented data collection and analysis system. This sub-component
will aim for some further MIS expansion, namely:
(a) integration of the MIS system and expansion to the district level with
computerization to allow better data analysis and quicker feedback to
PHC managers; and
(b) further development and use of the database, developed in 1989/90 with
the assistance of the ADB, so that additional district and provincial
data are maintained and can be used in decision making.
25.       On the financial side, at the provincial level, the annual sector
budget exercise is faced with the dilemma of inadequate and inappropriate
expenditure figures compounded by unsatisfactory mid-term revised estimates and



- 107 -
Annex 5
Page 10 of 10
improperly functioning reporting against approved PC forms. Through the project,
a provincial level analysis will be undertaken which will describe and appraise
the existing system, and recommend system and implementation improvements
including a program for their introduction. Similar changes, as appropriate,
will subsequently be implemented at the district level and below, linked with the
other district management development activities. Also, as a short-term step,
a simple bi-annual financial reporting system will be introduced, both for the
development and recurrent budgets, to facilitate sectoral financial monitoring.
Transport and Communications
26.       Each DHDC will be provided with one vehicle for supervision and
collecting staff for in-service training programs. Appropriate POL and TA/DA
will also be provided in order to facilitate the visiting program to health
facilities, distribution of materials and education activities.
(F) Involvement of Other Parties in Health System Management
27.       While a final decision on whether District Health Boards would be
formed at the district level has yet to be taken, it is expected that any such
body would be an advisory and would review progress, make broad recommendations
for health care development and seek public support. Health status indicators
and the system's delivery would be reviewed at meetings. The District Health
Officer would be responsible for the initiation and review of the district's
health development for the Board. The District Health Boards should probably
include, inter alia, staff from the district administration, women's
organizations, several health staff including a representative of female health
workers, representatives from the Population Welfare Department and the local
government health system and representatives of private practitioners.
28.       Inter-sectoral collaboration could also evolve from the communities'
demands at the Union Council level. The health manager would need to attempt to
facilitate development in other sectors as well as negotiate for other or
additional resources from the DHO's office. Potential inter-sectoral programs
include water supply, education of females, and control of use of dangerous
psychotropic drugs.
29.       The complexity of logistics and the size of the different districts,
especially in Balochistan, necessitate more decentralization of decision making
and more involvement of the public in planning public health services. This will
be achieved through:
(a) Following increased management capacity, the implementation of
decentralization of assignments to lower levels. This will include
financial and personnel management.
(b) Decentralization of management will be combined with decentralization
of services: the BHU will be made the center of PHC in the Union
Council and provide as comprehensive services as possible, including
a mini-laboratory, according to circumstances and staffing.
